## 560

560

By the Grace of Hashem, 11 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Rabbi, Chassid, G-d-fearing, learned in Torah and Chassidus, engaged in communal affairs, etc., R’ Shlomo Yosef, may he live.

Peace and Blessing!

Your letter has been received.

In anticipation of the Festival of Matzos, the season of our freedom, which is approaching for us and for all of Israel for good, I hereby express my blessing for a kosher and joyous holiday and for true freedom—freedom from material worries and spiritual worries—from everything that hinders the service of Hashem with joy and a good heart.

And may this freedom be drawn into all the days of the entire year.

Especially since the service of Hashem, as commanded to us by our Torah—the Torah of life—encompasses all of a person’s affairs throughout the entire day and night, as it is said, "In all your ways know Him." See carefully the beginning of the fifth chapter of the Rambam’s *Shemonah Perakim*.

With holiday blessings,

**M. Schneerson**

*Pelah HaRimon* on *Shir HaShirim*—as far as I know, it is extremely rare. (There is one copy in the library of the Rebbe, my father-in-law, of righteous memory). Also, the letters are not clear, etc.

The book *Sippurei Chassidim* (for the holidays), *LaOr HaHalacha*—as of now, they have not been received.

Regarding Mr. ... may he live—it is understood that I spoke about **several** matters that are more important than the issue of the building for Tiferes Bachurim.

And the fact that he did not mention them—apparently, it is because from a partisan point of view, they are not "popular."

Regarding what you wrote about the interpretation of the Name *Havayah*: Three views are brought there: 1) *Mehaveh* (time and place). 2) *Hayah, Hoveh, v’Yihyeh* simultaneously (transcending time and place). [Both are in *Zohar* Vol. III, 257, bottom of side b; *Pardes Rimonim*, Gate 1, Ch. 9; in *Shaar HaYichud v’HaEmunah*—by the Alter Rebbe, *P’ri Etz Chaim*; and in many other places.] 3) It has no explanation at all (*Pardes Rimonim*, Gate 19, Ch. 1). And in general, it is: *Memaleh Kol Almin*, *Sovev Kol Almin*, and transcending both *Memaleh* and *Sovev*—vessel, light, and essence. And according to what is written in *Ginas Egoz*—and explained in the *Shelah* at the beginning, in the *Maamar* "Kol HaShoneh" and the *Maamar* "Ein Omdin" of the *Hemshech* 5666, in the *Maamarim* for Pesach and Shavuos 5668, and elsewhere—it is understood that it exists—it is the *Havayah* that is not pronounced and also **not** written, contrary to the opinion of the Gra.

Regarding what remains difficult in my opinion (in the supplement to the *Haggadah Shel Pesach*) concerning the second reason of the *Abudraham*—it is not that one does not fulfill the obligation (for regarding this, he noted that one fulfills the obligation of *Shehecheyanu* after the fact, and it would have been better to cite the blessing of the *mitzvos* in the *Sukkah* (*Orach Chaim*, end of Ch. 639)), but rather—that one must have *kavanah*. And this, after all, does not apply to *Bedikas Chametz* and *Sukkah*—for there is no room for *kavanah* regarding what has already passed (and also, one cannot recite a blessing over a secondary matter).

Regarding what you wrote concerning the *Shehecheyanu* of *Mishloach Manos*—in *Sefer Yaavetz*, he writes that one should have in mind during the *Shehecheyanu* blessing also the *Mishloach Manos*, etc., and one who does not have a *Megillah* should also recite *Shehecheyanu*.

Regarding your question on the *Tzafenas Paneach*—the words of the *Tzafenas Paneach* are on the words of the Rambam, who writes a difficulty regarding them—and he distinguishes between the time of the *Beis HaMikdash* and the present time.

## 561

B"H, 11 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings!

In anticipation of the festival of Matzos, the season of our freedom, which is approaching us and all of Israel for the good, I hereby express my blessing for a kosher and joyous holiday and for true freedom—freedom from material worries and spiritual worries, from everything that hinders the service of Hashem with joy and a glad heart.

And may this freedom and joy be drawn into all the days of the entire year.

Especially since the service of Hashem, as we are commanded in our Torah—the Torah of life—encompasses all of a person’s affairs throughout the entire day and night, as it is written: "In all your ways, know Him."

With holiday blessings,

**M. Schneerson**

According to the reports reaching here, it appears that it has been a very long time since you have withdrawn from communal activity, as has been your custom for some time (with the exception of publishing books). The astonishment regarding this most regrettable and painful phenomenon is understandable. Does it truly seem to you now that this is the opportune time to flee from the battlefield? Or is it your opinion that the generation has improved? The notion that desirable communal activity is currently unsuccessful, and that propaganda for yiras shamayim and Torah and mitzvos is ineffective, is absolutely refuted by reality. Especially since who among us can measure the hearts of the children of Israel, that they would, chas v'shalom, not listen to the voice of one who rebukes them according to the way of the Torah, even if he speaks to them early and late? As our Sages of blessed memory said, "You shall surely rebuke"—even up to a hundred times, provided the words come from the heart. What is certain is that the absence of communal activity will certainly add nothing to improving the situation of Yiddishkeit and Torah and mitzvos.

There was a terrible and dire situation, rachmana litzlan, in the days of Yechezkel (chapters 8-9), the likes of which had not been seen since the Men of the Great Assembly (Yoma 69b). And yet, the story of our Sages (Shabbos 55a) is well known, which, even upon a slight contemplation of it, would make the hair of a Jewish person stand on end.

Perhaps the style is not sufficiently diplomatic or polite—but surely the sharpness is not at all commensurate with the trouble and distress in which the matters of Yiddishkeit find themselves everywhere.

#### ***561***

Printed in Likkutei Sichos, Vol. 12, p. 203, and completed based on a photocopy of the letter.

## 5362

13 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed Rabbi Zvi, who is involved in communal affairs with energy, etc.

Greetings and blessings!

I received your letter of the 9th of Adar Sheni, along with the enclosed reprint, which I read with interest. Due to the magnitude of my preoccupations, my response has been delayed.

I wish to emphasize a primary point that ought to serve as the foundation for any debate, yet it is surprising that it is not emphasized as it should be. Even in your reprint, it is mentioned only as a hint, and at the very end, and in a brief manner where it would have been appropriate to elaborate.

The point is this: In any debate, whatever it may be, one must begin from certain premises that are shared by both sides. Only then does the debate have a purpose—meaning, if one side can convince the other based on these premises that the other has also agreed to, then the other side is compelled to accept the conclusions that follow from them. If, however, there are no shared premises, what benefit is there for one to argue based on assumptions that do not compel the other side? Each party simply insists on its own position, it is impossible to reach common ground, and the entire debate is in vain.

So it is with any debate regarding Yiddishkeit. First of all, one must establish the foundation for the discussion one wishes to conduct; if this premise is accepted, then it necessitates the conclusions. This premise is either based on the history of the Jewish people from then until now, in the sense of physical survival; or based on the preservation of spiritual values that were transmitted in a specific manner by specific individuals; or based on the success of our people in avoiding assimilation throughout all the years of exile, and the like. Very often, when one establishes a certain premise at the outset that the other side must also acknowledge, one thereby strips them of their basis and refutes their arguments in advance.

In the present case, for example, if one begins the debate with a statement that cannot be denied—namely, that Yiddishkeit is connected to a society of people called Jews—then in order to define what "Yiddishkeit" is, one must find the essential matter and the common denominator that makes Jews "Jews." If we review the history of our people over the course of three thousand five hundred years, since we became a nation, and all that we have endured in different places and under conditions ranging from one extreme to the other—both regarding the spiritual environment and the physical environment—we are **compelled** to conclude that it was not the language, nor the land, nor a specific territory, nor even a philosophical system, that united the Jews and identified them (and ensured their survival). Rather, the only thing that did not change, and which is the common denominator in all places and under all conditions, is the observance of *mitzvos ma'asiyos*. Since this is the historical reality, any researcher whose desires do not mislead his intellect must recognize the truth as it is, in accordance with reality—even if he cannot grasp the matter with his intellect, or if the conclusions are not to his liking and desire, or are not in accordance with the worldview of a certain person, or are not in the spirit of a certain philosophical system. For reality necessitates the conclusions, and preconceived conclusions do not change reality.

Another point, which in my opinion was not emphasized enough or was mentioned only as a hint, is the well-known parable that a person cannot lift himself by pulling on the hair of his own head. Likewise, it is contrary to sound logic to pick and choose from *Torah Elokis* according to one's own will—to reject a part of the Torah that does not suit one's intellect and desire, while affirming another part of the Torah. That is to say, by his own will, he invalidates one part of the Torah, while regarding another part, he says it is above his will and intellect, insofar as he accepts that part as a Divine matter.

I will add one more point, even though it has no direct connection to your reprint: there are many matters whose entire existence comes only from the fact that one wages war against them. Were it not for this war, they would be forgotten and would cease to exist of their own accord, since they have no content or existence in and of themselves. It seems this is the case with the Reconstructionist movement; despite the fact that they have much money, their primary existence comes from the fact that one wages a vigorous war against them.

A matter parallel to the above is the distinction between the teachings of Chassidus and the teachings of *Mussar*. The teachings of Chassidus emphasize the matter of *aseh tov* (doing good), as the constant *avodah* along the line of *sur meira* (turning away from evil) gives, to a certain extent, a foothold to evil. And although until the prophecy is fulfilled, "And I will remove the spirit of impurity from the earth," it is necessary to also engage in the line of *sur meira*, nevertheless, as much as possible, one should avoid making this *avodah* the goal and content of most of his days, and instead occupy himself with the line of *aseh tov*; this is more praiseworthy.

I have already said several times that one of the advantages of America compared to Europe in the past is that the situation here is not the presence of evil that one must fight, but primarily *am-ha'aratzus* (ignorance), which is merely a matter of absence and negation, and which has led to a spiritual vacuum and a lack of content. Therefore, the line of *aseh tov* is even more required here, and all the debates are of secondary, perhaps tertiary, or even lower importance.

Primarily, one must disseminate upright views based on the Torah and the *mesorah*. In these days in particular, in order for this outreach to be accepted by wide circles, and especially among the youth, it must be permeated with light, vitality, and a movement of joy—which are also characteristics and foundations of the Chassidic approach.

With the blessing that you recognize your inner role to make the center where you serve a center of springs of living waters of our Torah—the Torah of life, the Torah of truth—for truth is the negation of all compromises. May you fulfill your role with an expansion of mind, and with the blessing of a *kasher* and happy *Pesach*.

## 563

13 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn

To the Avrech Shalom Leib, may he live:

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 29 Adar Sheni and those preceding it.

Regarding what you write concerning the difficulties in obtaining tefillin (seemingly, it warrants investigation why you do not also mention the expenses for checking tefillin; in any case, when the chain of transmission of the tefillin is not clear before they come into your possession, and) you may inform Tzeirei Agudas Chabad that for every pair of tefillin they manage to provide for you, I will, G-d willing, encourage Anash to provide a second pair. This means that the same effort and money paid for one pair of tefillin will effectively secure two pairs. Keep me updated from time to time on the results, and I will then encourage the provision of the necessary funds from the special campaign fund.

Incidentally, the custom here is to take tefillin found in shuls, since no one is claiming them. In most cases, they are collected from tefillin that heirs bring from the deceased and leave in the shul. After they are checked, they should of course be used.

2) Regarding what you wrote about how to answer the argument of those who say that it is permitted to kindle fire on Shabbos, and that the prohibition applies only when one needs to rub stone against stone, as is the custom among primitive tribes, whereas the method of lighting fire in our times is not connected to any labor.

It is understood that the primary argument is the one you wrote in your letter: that we must fulfill the Mitzvos of the Creator without asking for their rationale. This is especially true since it is understood even with simple logic that it is impossible for a created intellect to understand and grasp the intellect of the Creator, as there is no comparison between the created and the Creator. This is a *kal v'chomer*: If it is obvious to everyone that a small child beginning to learn Aleph-Beis cannot understand a deep *sugya* in the Gemara or the reasoning of the Rishonim—even though they are both in the category of human beings who possess a rational soul—and furthermore, we know that when the child grows up and fulfills the study through toil, it is said of him, "I have toiled and I have found," meaning that the child’s intellect has the *potential* to grasp even the deep reasoning in the Gemara, but it has not yet been brought into actuality—then how much more so regarding matters of the Creator in relation to matters of the created, which cannot be compared to one another, as one is the Creator and the other is the created. The reasons found in the Torah explain only a small part of the matters and roots of the Mitzvah, which is why there are many different reasons and facets for every matter and Mitzvah in the Torah.

Beyond all this, even according to logic, there is *no* room at all for the aforementioned reasoning and difficulty, both from the perspective of reality and from the perspective of logic.

3) From the perspective of reality: At the time of the Giving of the Torah—that is, at the Exodus of the Children of Israel from the land of Egypt—it is known in all history books that the culture and technical matters in the land of Egypt were quite advanced. Furthermore, there are several details and matters (in the manner of transporting loads, construction, mixing colors, and more) that even *now* we do not know how to do, or how they did them in those days. Specifically, the kindling of fire among the Egyptians was not done by rubbing stone against stone, but rather through several easier methods. In the vast majority of cases, they would kindle *fire from fire*, as is recounted in the chronicles of the land of Egypt and its surroundings in those days. This was especially true in the houses of the Egyptian sorcerers, which also served as places of learning for the sciences known at that time, which, as mentioned, were advanced in several details. In this, even the method of kindling fire (which was connected to several forms of idol worship at the time) stood at the highest level—that is, with *minimal* effort.

4) From the perspective of logic: The observance of Shabbos according to the simple meaning of the verse is because "in six days Hashem made the heavens and the earth, and on the seventh day He rested." It is simple to any person of intellect that the Holy One, Blessed be He, created His world without toil or labor. And from the act of the work of the six days of Creation—from the *manner* of this work, which is not connected to any labor at all—He rested on the seventh day, and in this example, the Children of Israel were also commanded.

5) In general, regarding arguments such as the above: the argument itself proves that the one making it believes that the Torah was given by the Creator of the world, but he wishes to seek to interpret the intent of the Creator and His command as applying only under different, specific conditions and in certain eras. This argument itself is proven to be false *from within itself*. Since the Torah was given by the Creator of the world for the benefit of man—as the phrasing goes, "for they are our life and the length of our days"—

a. *For the benefit* of man [and this too is a necessity, for one who believes in the Creator believes that the Creator is good and upright, and therefore we are obligated to fulfill His command], this negates the possibility that the Torah would be given in a way that misleads man, G-d forbid, or that gives room for a fundamental doubt—that is, that the intent was only for a certain period, and when the time changes, the command will change. Yet, the Author of the Torah wrote the opposite of this [as is proven from several verses that the commands of the Torah are "as the days of the heavens upon the earth," and also during the time of exile until our righteous Mashiach comes, as all these matters are mentioned several times in the Torah]. There is not *one* statement that explains that before this, the command of the Torah will change, G-d forbid, and it is obvious that there is no explanation as to in which direction the changes would be or in what manner.

As you requested, I will mention you for what you need, and regarding the matters you wrote about, at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory. May it be His will that you utilize the days between now and then for all that is required to spread the wellsprings in the environment where you are located, and in a way that your recipients will also "moisten" others in the environment where they find themselves, even after time has passed.

With the blessing of a kosher and happy Pesach,

**M. Schneerson**

## 564

B"H, 13 Nissan, 5717

In response to your letter containing questions regarding the matter of "Atah Lomar" (You say), it is surprising that you do not inform me of your study schedule. Most importantly, [I would like to know] whether you are saying it with consistency and diligence, and whether you are conducting yourself as a Jewish avreich ought to conduct himself in the study of Torah, in the diligent fulfillment of mitzvos in general, and in the honor of parents and teachers, and so on. On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

A. Quint
Secretary

**When the Mishkan traveled, which of the Leviim carried the laver (kiyor) and its base?**

**Response:** It is not mentioned that the laver and its base had poles, and without poles, it is impossible to carry them on the shoulder, but rather [they were carried] in a wagon. This is also written in the Sefer Chizkuni (Shemos 30:18). In the Beraisa of the Work of the Mishkan, Chapter 13, it is written: The families of the Gershuni were appointed over all the vessels in the Mishkan (besides those carried by the sons of Kehas, which are mentioned in the Beraisa before this). The sons of Merari were appointed over the planks, etc. From this, it is implied that the sons of Gershon carried the laver and its base.

**Why is it not explicitly stated in the text who carried them?**

**Response:** Because the purpose of these vessels was not for their own sake, but rather to prepare the Kohanim for the avodah. (This is also the reason why the command of Hashem to make them was not given at the time He commanded the making of the other vessels; see there in the Chizkuni).

## 566

B"H, 13 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the students of the vocational school in Kfar Chabad, may Hashem protect them and keep them alive.

Peace and blessing!

In recent days, the table crafted by your hands was received, which is something to be proud of, in addition to the Menorah that you made, which was received some time ago.

As I look at and contemplate these vessels, my hope is strong that you are doing everything within your power in the two lines of the table and the Menorah—both good and refined—namely, the material and the spiritual. This is in accordance with the well-known statement of our Sages: "One who wishes to become wise should turn to the south, and one who wishes to become wealthy should turn to the north," and your mnemonic is: the table in the north and the Menorah in the south.

Regarding the affairs of students in a yeshiva and school, the material and the table represent the fulfillment of mitzvos that are dependent upon material matters, while the spiritual and the Menorah represent the study of Torah, the wisdom of the Ribono shel Olam.

And regarding both—in the study of Torah and in the beauty and holiness of the fulfillment of mitzvos—our Sages have said: "If you have labored and found, believe it," and with the additional promise: "A person sanctifies himself a little from below, and they sanctify him much from above."

And since you have merited to study in a yeshiva where the revealed Torah—the "body of the Torah"—is united with the hidden Torah—the "soul of the Torah"—may it be His will that you be healthy in body and in soul, and that you increase your study of Torah with diligence and persistence, and your fulfillment of mitzvos with beauty. May the Giver of the Torah and the mitzvos bless you and your entire families, from soul to flesh.

With blessings for success and for a kosher and happy Pesach.

## 5637

B"H, 13 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and distinguished individual, occupied with communal needs,
R' Naftali, may he live, HaKohen

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 23 Adar II and those preceding it.

In general, my opinion is that you should continue to study in the place where you are currently learning, for several reasons—especially since you also write that they have stopped talking about your studies, and if so, etc. Regarding what you wrote—that because of your involvement in the evening yeshivah, your arrival is slightly delayed—you must seek solutions for this. I mean, perhaps there is someone else who could take over for that short time you currently spend at the evening yeshivah. Because of the arrangements that must be maintained at the yeshivah where you study, perhaps someone else could substitute for you at the evening yeshivah. Surely it is not so difficult if you look into it, and there will be no loss to the students of the evening yeshivah, as it is surely only for a short while. If, however, it is impossible to arrange both, it is worthwhile that you continue your studies at your current yeshivah. The merit of your involvement in the evening yeshivah, and in the dissemination of the wellsprings in general, will stand by you, in accordance with the clear promise of our Rebbe, the Alter Rebbe, that one’s mind and heart become a thousand times more refined. This promise is strengthened by the unique quality of these days, for the entire month is in the category of Rosh Chodesh, as brought in the Shelah and explained in Chassidus. The entirety of this month is the month of redemption and miracles from all matters that confuse, conceal, and obscure. True freedom is that which is connected to our Torah, and specifically to the inner dimension of the Torah, which relates more to the letters of the engraving—*charut* (engraved) upon the Tablets. May it be Hashem’s will that you see all of this tangibly, and that you bring good tidings in all matters, both general and personal.

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach.

## 568

B"H, 13 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter from Erev Rosh Chodesh Nissan, in which you write about your son, the avreich [name]—may he live and be well—who is being presented with distinguished proposals, but who, according to your letter, does not wish to interest himself in a shidduch, claiming that he wants to dedicate more time to the study of Torah without having a "millstone around his neck," etc.

It is understood that there is no place to force anyone to accept a **specific** proposal regarding shidduchim. On the other hand, there is room to say that your son’s aforementioned argument stems from the fact that the proposals being presented to him, in his opinion, are not suitable; and if he were presented with something suitable, his perspective on the matter as a whole would change.

In any case, according to what is explained in the Laws of Talmud Torah by our Alter Rebbe, and especially according to the custom of Jerusalem and your family, it is appropriate that you exert the necessary energy to find suitable proposals for your son—may he live and be well—and present them to him. May it be Hashem’s will that this be successful, and that he will continue to study Torah in purity—both the revealed Torah and the teachings of Chassidus—even after his wedding, and that he will be successful.

With blessings for Chassidishe nachas from all your offspring—may they live—and for a kosher and happy Pesach holiday.

## 566

566

B"H, 13 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

Following a pause, I received your letter in which you write that people are speaking to you about a shidduch, but your soul desires Torah and you wish to travel to a yeshiva in another location to study there for some time, and not to enter into a shidduch until then. You ask for my opinion on this.

Now, according to what is explained in the Hilchos Talmud Torah of the Alter Rebbe, and especially according to the custom of Jerusalem, the Holy City—which I am certain your family also follows—it does not seem appropriate to postpone interest in shidduch matters. In such cases, "custom is Torah." However, it is understood that the proposal must be such that both sides agree in peace that even after the wedding, you will continue to study in tranquility. Indeed, many people enter into shidduchim in this manner and are successful thereafter.

Regarding what you wrote—that the learning of an unmarried person is not comparable to the learning of a married person—see the aforementioned Hilchos Talmud Torah. Primarily, based on what is known from experience, it is a matter of debate whether the difference is for the better or for the worse. However, in many places, people change [their conduct] because a poor custom has taken root, where immediately after the wedding, one’s head and entire being are invested in worldly matters, business, and the like. Since in the Holy Land, in Jerusalem, the upright custom from earlier times has been preserved—to study after the wedding, to the point that this is accepted as a matter of course even by the women—it is understood that it is not worthwhile for you to break this fence, chas v'shalom. Especially since I am confident regarding you that it is better for you to enter into a shidduch as soon as possible, and Hashem will grant you success, in accordance with the verse, "And from Hashem is an intelligent wife." You will then be able to study the revealed Torah and Chassidus in purity, both together, and in Jerusalem, the Holy City, may it be rebuilt and established.

I trust you are participating in the spreading of the wellsprings and are increasing this from time to time, which, in addition to the primary importance of the matter, is also a segulah for an increase in the blessings of Hashem in all that a person requires for his affairs.

With blessings for a kosher and happy Pesach.

## 570

B"H, 13 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter from the 29th of Adar II, in which you write your personal thoughts regarding your future arrangements,

It is surprising that you do not mention the arrangement that should be the most immediate: that you should study at the Tomchei Temimim Yeshiva for at least another year or two. After all, it was for this purpose that you went to Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt and established), and your success in this depends solely on your own will, for the ability has been granted to you. It is understood that this study can be connected to a specific goal, such as receiving *semicha* or the like. This does not necessarily require a decision to serve in a formal rabbinic capacity, but rather serves to strengthen the recognition that you are succeeding in your studies and making progress, and that you will be able to influence your future environment with conduct appropriate to the instruction of our Torah, the Torah of life.

After you complete the aforementioned studies successfully, then you should look into arrangements in Kfar Chabad or in a *chareidi* *kibbutz*. Together with the physical work in the *kibbutz*, you should be the "living spirit" there in matters of Yiddishkeit and Chassidus. *V'kal* (and this is simple to understand). May it be the will of Hashem that you decide on all of the above as is proper, and then you will see that the difficulties that currently seem to stand in the way of realizing this are, for the most part, merely imagined.

With blessings for good news and a kosher and happy Pesach.

**5170**

**To the Avreich Naftali Tzvi, may he live:** Gottlieb, Jerusalem.

**Recitation of the psalm "Lamnatzeach":** [It is recited] at a time when Tachanun is not said, even if the reason Tachanun was not said is not according to Chabad custom. See also above, Letter 5131.

**Regarding your second question:** When praying in a synagogue where Tachanun is not said on a yahrtzeit, which is not according to our customs, [the question is] whether to say Tachanun (which sometimes involves friction and arguments) or not to be separated from the congregation and not say Tachanun.

## 5137

B"H, 17 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi, man of action, G-d-fearing and learned, our teacher Rabbi Aharon, may he live:

Shalom u’Bracha!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter in which you write regarding the possibility of arranging poultry shechita. I assume you mean in Jerusalem, the Holy City—may it be rebuilt and established—and its surroundings, or in any event, in a place where one will not be isolated. Therefore, the logic behind this arrangement seems sound to me. May it be the will of Hashem that it be successful, both materially and spiritually, and may you utilize your talents to strengthen Yiddishkeit and disseminate it in your surroundings now.

This is contrary to those who mistakenly claim that the challenges to Yiddishkeit exist only in our former country, and that beyond the borders of that land, there is no need for awakening and mesiras nefesh. For, unfortunately, we see that the challenge of lacking persecution—the freedom for every person to do as they see fit—is also a difficult test. And since you are a man who has seen affliction, having witnessed how people stood with mesiras nefesh for the study of Torah and the fulfillment of mitzvos amidst terrible decrees, surely your words will be heard more effectively when you inspire those around you now regarding the study of Torah with yiras shamayim and the fulfillment of practical mitzvos in their literal sense, as they should be, and so on. And whoever sustains even one soul of Yisrael is as if he has sustained an entire world.

With blessings for good tidings in all the aforementioned.

Due to the sanctity of the Yom Tov, the Rebbe shlita has not signed, and I am signing on his behalf.

## 5778

5778

B”H, 17 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rav, G-d-fearing, man of action, possessing fine character traits and energy, etc.,
Rabbi Yehuda Aryeh, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

After such a long hiatus, it was a pleasure to receive regards regarding your activities through the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, man of action, involved in communal needs, Rabbi Chodakov, may he live. It was good to learn that you have returned to your strength and are continuing your activities for the strengthening of Yiddishkeit and its dissemination, both in writing and orally. Surely, for someone like yourself, it is unnecessary to point out the rejection of compromises—not only compromises regarding the actual fulfillment of mitzvos, for that is obvious, but even compromises regarding opinions, and even compromises in the explanation of matters where some say that science contradicts the Torah’s perspective, and they explain that this is the only way to win over youth who believe in science and its conclusions. Yet, we see that this approach has cast down many victims—not only victims regarding false opinions, but, G-d forbid, a weakening in the fulfillment of mitzvos, and certainly in the mesiras nefesh for the fulfillment of mitzvos.

It is precisely on this point that our generation—an orphaned generation—has merited an additional light: even men of science admit that all the conclusions of science are merely hypotheses. Even according to their own logic, one cannot challenge a certainty (*bari*) with a doubt (*shema*); that is to say, one cannot challenge a system whose essence is certainty—as are all matters of faith and religion—with a system that they themselves admit is only a hypothesis.

Especially in Europe, warnings against such compromises are necessary, following the destruction wrought by the method of apologetics. As is known, the "explanations" regarding the age of the world, the theory of evolution, etc., follow the well-known dictum of our Sages: "With his own axe, he cuts down his own tree." Since you have the pen in your hand, in the same language in which that aforementioned method was disseminated, and you recognize its nature, and you are located in a country that is saturated with "Chachmas Yisrael," etc., you have an additional merit to begin correcting the distortion and the deficiency, at least to the extent possible. May it be the will of Hashem that you succeed in this.

Enclosed is a copy of the general letter for the festival of Pesach, which I am sure will interest you.

With holiday blessings.

Due to the sanctity of the festival, the Rebbe shlita has not signed, and I am signing on his behalf,

The Secretary

## 579

B"H, 17 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

It is a proper thing to complete the craft of shechita, including shechita and bedika for both small and large livestock. As mentioned, it is most correct and good to know the craft of shechita and bedika in its entirety.

Surely you are aware of what is explained in several places, including by the Alter Rebbe and the Tzemach Tzedek, that a shochet u'bodek must be a yirei shamayim to the greatest possible degree—literally, a yirei shamayim in the three pillars of Torah, avodah, and gemilus chasadim. Those who have merited the light of Chassidus, in particular, must be permeated with the light and vitality of Chassidus. May Hashem grant you success.

In order to increase the siyata d'shmaya in this, one of the segulos is the matter of hafatzas hama'ayanos chutzah, and in general, bringing Bnei Yisrael closer to our Father in Heaven, and this is well understood.

With blessings for good tidings,

Due to the sanctity of the festival, the Rebbe shlita has not signed personally, and I sign on his behalf,

The Secretary

Printed in Likkutei Sichos, Vol. 17, p. 21.

B"H, 18 Nissan, 5717

## 583

B"H, 18 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings,

In response to your letter of 8 Nissan, in which you ask whether it is true, as some say, that regarding *zivugim* (matches) there is a need for compatibility, etc., and likewise regarding what is said about the wisdom of palmistry (*chachmas hayad*), and whether all the above contradicts the commandment, "You shall be wholehearted with Hashem your G-d."

The compatibility of *zivugim* and the wisdom of palmistry are discussed in the books of Kabbalah and *pnimiyus haTorah* (the inner dimension of Torah), and some of these sources are noted in the margin. From this, it is understood that this is not the opposite of the commandment, "You shall be wholehearted with Hashem your G-d," and it is similar to matters of guarding the body and soul, matters of medicine, proven *segulos*, and the like. See also *Shulchan Aruch*, *Yoreh Deah*, section 179, regarding several details of these matters.

However, this applies only when one knows these matters thoroughly—the roots of the *neshamos*, the lines of the hand, etc. But for one who does not have clear knowledge of this [which in our generation can only be attained through *kabbalah* (receiving tradition) from a master, and not through one's own intellect], it is obvious that regarding this, too, it is said, "Many are the slain she has cast down" (Mishlei 7:26)—this refers to one who has not reached the level of instruction yet presumes to instruct. I am surprised if there is anyone in our generation who would say and publicize that he is an expert in these matters.

It is my hope that you set times for the study of *Chassidus*, the inner dimension of the Torah, and distance yourself from matters of *kabbalah ma'asit* (practical Kabbalah) and its accessories, as the warning regarding this is well-known in many holy books.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

***

 *Zohar* Vol. III, 43b; ibid., 283b; *Zohar* Vol. II, 76a; and elsewhere.

 See Ramban on the Torah, *Bereishis* 5:2, and in the books noted in *Nitzutzei Zohar* to *Zohar* Vol. II, 70a.

## 580

B.H., 20 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and learned R’ Zev, may he live:

Shalom u’vrachah!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter dated 13 Nissan, the day of the Hillula of the Tzemach Tzedek and his predecessor.

I am writing to reply immediately, to assure you that your previous letter caused no offense whatsoever; in general, offense and matters of that nature are not my concern.

My hope is that by the time you receive this letter, your health has improved, and that you are continuing to increase the closeness of the children of Israel to our Father in Heaven through chinuch al taharas hakodesh—for there is nothing greater than this. At the same time, it is understood and simple, in accordance with the teaching "do not hide yourself from your own flesh," that one must also increase the fixed times for studying the revealed Torah and Chassidus, and strive to spread the wellsprings outward. Although, the order of this must be in accordance with what is written in the Laws of Talmud Torah of the Alter Rebbe, Chapter 1, sections 11 and 12. May Hashem grant you success in bringing good tidings regarding this.

Regarding what you wrote about the Shabbos meals—that there appear to be contradictions in the statements of the Zohar:

The order is as stated by the Rashbi (Zohar III, 88b), and as we say in *Atkinu Se'udata*: *Chakal Tapuchin* [corresponds to] *Atik Kadisha*, and *Ze'er Anpin*. As for what is written in Zohar III (88a)—"the first meal corresponds to *Atik Kadisha*..." and "the second meal corresponds to *Chakal Tapuchin*..."—this has already been explained in *Ohr HaChama* there, noting that it is explaining according to the order of the verses, not the order of the meals. Similarly, what is written in Zohar III, 288b (bottom)—is according to the level of the *Midos*—as explained in *Ohr HaChama* to Zohar III, 88b. And in the notes of the Alter Rebbe in his *Drushei Se'udos Shabbos*, the matter is explained according to Chassidus.

Regarding your question on Rashi on "Bagad Bagadta" (You have acted treacherously)—that she herself gave her to Yaakov—it can be explained simply: because he did not refuse. Furthermore, it can be explained according to the principle that even when a woman gives permission without being persuaded, she feels distressed in her heart (see *Nitzotzei Ketzarim* to *Shulchan Aruch Even HaEzer*, 76:6).

Regarding your question on the statement of our Sages (Bereishis Rabbah 71:5) that "And she conceived" is not said regarding Zilpah because she was a young girl—yet, in the birth of Pinchas, "And she conceived" is also not said. It is not understood how one can conclude that in the case of Pinchas she was not a young girl. See *Chizkuni* to Shemos 2:22. Seemingly, there is a difficulty from Shemos (6:20), where "And she conceived" is not said, even though she was 125 years old at the birth of Miriam. However, there is no difficulty from the outset, because the *Bereishis Rabbah* is precise regarding the change in language, as it states: "**In all of them** 'conception' is mentioned, **except [for this one]**." The interpretation of the *Chizkuni* must be considered merely an *asmachta* (a supporting allusion).

Regarding your question on the change between Uel and Reuel—this, too, is not understood, as there are many people in Scripture who had more than one name, such as Yisro and Chovav.

With blessings for good tidings.

Due to the holiness of the festival, the Rebbe shlita has not signed personally, and I sign on his behalf,

The Secretary

## 587

B"H, 20 Nissan, 5717

Greetings and blessings!

It is a great wonder and a cause for concern that since your wedding, there has been no word from you. Although my hope is strong that you are fulfilling the directive that "each and every day one must perform his work," especially within the framework of *hafatzas hama'ayanos chutzah* and the framework of *Tzeirei Agudas Chabad*. You should certainly write with the necessary details regarding all of the above, as well as how the days of the wedding and the seven days of feasting passed.

And although there is room to say that the days before the wedding, as well as the wedding itself and the days following it, involve additional preoccupation, it is understood that a sensible person neither needs to nor can allow these preoccupations to distract him from the aforementioned activities, or even from reporting on them—on the contrary, and *kal v'chomer* [it is obvious]. See also the *Hilchos Talmud Torah* of our Alter Rebbe, Chapter 1, Paragraph 9, for even after marriage one can study for two or three years without great distraction, etc.

With blessings.

A. Quint

## 588

B"H, 20 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter from the eve of our Festival of Freedom, in which you write that the shiurim are organized and ongoing.

Certainly, for one such as yourself, it is superfluous to emphasize that a person is called a "walker" [one who travels], since we are commanded to "ascend in holiness" and not be satisfied with the state of the day before yesterday, or even of yesterday and this morning. For every single day must "perform its work." You should also examine the wonderful explanation of this according to Chassidus in the Iggeres HaKodesh of our Alter Rebbe, Epistle 14. This applies in the finest detail to every single day; study it well. And since the Ribono shel Olam only demands according to one's capacity, surely the strength has been granted to fulfill this pleasant command.

In the above, there is also an answer to the conclusion of your letter regarding what the end and ultimate purpose of one's efforts will be, etc. For there is no better purpose than bringing the hearts of Bnei Yisrael closer to our Father in Heaven through the dissemination of the wellsprings outward—the inwardness of the Torah, the *sestim d'oraysa* (the hidden aspects of Torah), **which** connects even the hidden aspect of the neshamah with the hidden aspect of the Ribono shel Olam. And this is understood.

May it be the will [of Hashem] that our Festival of Freedom also influence you with freedom from all things that distract from the avodah of Hashem, which must be present throughout the entire year and in all matters, as explained in the concept of "In all your ways know Him," in the Tur Shulchan Aruch Orach Chaim, section 231, and in the Shemonah Perakim of the Rambam, beginning of chapter 5. Study them well.

With blessings for good tidings.

Due to the holiness of the festival, the Rebbe shlita has not signed, and I am signing on his behalf,

The Secretary

With blessings,

## 590

B"H, 24 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed and distinguished individual, active in communal affairs, a man of many accomplishments, R' Meir Shalom, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

After such a long hiatus, it was a pleasure to receive your letter in which you write regarding... and the success that Divine Providence has granted you with Mr. Y.D., may he live—this will surely add further vigor to your efforts in the aforementioned endeavor. In such matters, the magnitude of one's merit is beyond description or estimation.

I am interested to know if Mr. Y.D. has shown you my recent letters to him, his state of mind, the environment in which he is settling, and so on.

Among all the matters, Mr. Y.D. wrote that, relying on my suggestion, he has also begun to speak with those he influences about the observance of practical mitzvos. Judging by the tone of his writing, his heart still hesitates as to whether his listeners are already prepared for such discussions, and he also fears that they might distance themselves as a result, etc., and perhaps there is further apprehension on his part, etc. Therefore, this is an opportunity for you to take advantage of a near occasion to steer the conversation with him toward this topic, without it being felt that the prompting came from me, and to encourage and strengthen him from your side as well regarding this matter. In this, too, the merit of the many will assist him.

It was a pleasure to read at the end of your letter about the improvement appearing in your livelihood. My hope is strong that this is only the beginning, and that the situation will improve even further, in a way that will allow you to dedicate more and more of your time and energy to spreading the wellsprings outward.

Enclosed is my letter for the holiday of Pesach to Mr. Avigdor Hameiri, may he live. Although the sending was delayed due to circumstances, in truth, the time for it is all year round. This is especially so according to the saying of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, that Chassidim do not say "Chasal Siddur Pesach." The Rebbe—our Alter Rebbe—did not include the words "Chasal Siddur Pesach" in the Siddur after the Haggadah, because in Chabad, Pesach does not end; rather, it is drawn out continuously. Surely, if the aforementioned individual requires it, you will add your own explanation. May it be His will that you succeed in fanning the Divine spark within him until it becomes a "flame rising of its own accord," as is known from our Sages that this is the goal of "When you kindle the lamps."

With blessings for good tidings in all the aforementioned,

**M. Schneerson**

## 591

591

B”H, 24 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

I received your letter in due time, in which you describe the farbrengen of Yud-Tes Kislev, the Rosh Hashanah of Chassidus, and your inner struggle regarding the preparations for this auspicious day, and how, in your opinion, not everything that was planned came to fruition, etc.

I will not hide from you that it gladdened me to read of your struggle regarding the preparations for the farbrengen. Based on the well-known teaching of our Sages, “If one says, ‘I have labored and not found,’ do not believe him,” [it is clear that] if these words apply to the personal affairs of an individual, how much more so when it concerns the many, and regarding a day that is not merely a private day, but a general one—the head of the entire year, as is the day mentioned. It is also understood that one should not be discouraged by a lack of complete self-satisfaction with the manner in which the farbrengen was conducted. On the contrary: until the time arrives of which it is said, “Then our mouth will be **filled** with laughter,” there is no perfection in the world. Anyone who claims to have **full** self-satisfaction from any holy endeavor is either deceiving himself, deceiving others, or is not speaking of the *neshamah Elokis* but of the *nefesh habahamit*—or something even lower than that, and the wise will understand.

Regarding what you wrote—that the farbrengen was not as successful as it could have been—those are not the reports that have reached me from others. On the contrary, the reports speak of success, *hisorerus*, and *romemus haruach*.

As for your point that no new public *shiurim* in *Toras HaChassidus* were established as a result of the farbrengen—a farbrengen can exert its influence in other ways. Furthermore, even when a farbrengen is successful to the highest degree, *avodah* is still required to bring the resolutions into the reality of daily life. If there is a lack in this *avodah*, there is a corresponding lack in the realization of the resolutions, and it is possible that the deficiency lies there, rather than in the farbrengen itself.

It is understood and simple that after all the above, I do not mean to say that it was impossible for the farbrengen to have been more refined and desirable. I only wish to emphasize that not only is there no reason for this to cause the depression and feelings you describe in your letter—to flee from these matters and become only a listener and a recipient—but, in addition to the primary point that this is the opposite of our holy Torah, which contains the great principle, “Love your fellow as yourself,” it is also the opposite of the specific mandate of our current era in general, and in Eretz Yisrael in particular, to spread the wellsprings outward. Everyone who has merited to taste from the wellsprings of Chassidus is obligated in this at every available opportunity. Moreover, one must also create opportunities if it seems to him that none are ready at hand, and the wise will understand.

My hope is strong that these few lines will suffice to uproot from the start the idea you wrote about—fleeing from the battlefield. Although the *sicha* is well known that one cannot be a *mashpia* without first being a recipient, on the other hand, people of your caliber and in your location must be *mashpiim*. And *mashpiim* specifically to the many, not only in Chassidic guidance but also in *Toras HaChassidus*. They are assured that they will succeed in this holy *avodah*, even if the reward is not always immediately apparent.

I hope that you also utilized the days of Pesach and the *yomei depagra* in the direction mentioned above. May Hashem grant you and your wife success in increasing activities of this nature, and through this, may you be blessed with Hashem’s blessings in your personal matters, and in the central point—to raise all your children to Torah, *chuppah*, and good deeds, in the light and vitality of Chassidus.

With blessings for good tidings.

P.S.
I am sure you participated in the gathering of *anash* in recent days, and I look forward to hearing good news regarding that as well.

## 5393

B"H, 24 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

I have received the news regarding your state of health, and I hope that from day to day you are seeing improvement in this. Certainly, for someone like you, it is unnecessary to emphasize the command of our holy Torah, the Torah of life, which grants the physician permission to heal. From this, it is understood that one must fulfill the instructions of the doctor. Even though at times the thought may arise that one is wasting time on matters of medical treatment, rest, and the like, even a slight contemplation makes it clear, based on the aforementioned statement of our Sages, that this is not a waste. On the contrary: by spending a short time that—to the physical eye—appears to be wasted, one gains much more time afterward, which one can then utilize with joy and a good heart to serve Hashem, "In all your ways know Him."

With blessings for a speedy recovery and for good tidings in all the aforementioned.

## 5394

B"H, 24 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter from Chol Hamoed Pesach, in which you write about your desire to study Torah, to walk in its ways, and to fulfill its mitzvos, and note that you encounter difficulties in bringing this desire to fruition, and you ask my opinion on the matter.

It is surprising that you even ask about this, for every one of us within Klal Yisrael is commanded to fulfill the will of the Creator, He who gives the Torah and the mitzvah. When one truly desires to fulfill it, one succeeds. And when one explains this desire in ways of pleasantness and ways of peace, without entering into conflict, surely even those who are not satisfied with this will eventually agree to the aforementioned conduct. For this is the command of Hashem, the Giver of the Torah, Who created man and knows what is for man’s benefit, and promises him in the language of the verse: "If you walk in My statutes and keep My mitzvos... I will provide your rains..." and all the blessings written in that parsha. May Hashem grant you success in bringing good news regarding all that has been said.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 595

B"H, 24 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To the esteemed Rabbi, G-d-fearing, learned, and diligent in communal needs, who sits in judgment, etc.,
Rabbi Yosef, may he live and be well,

Peace and blessing!

Your letter was received in due time, and due to the magnitude of the burden and the pressures, the response was delayed; I ask for your forgiveness.

Regarding what you write—that you have encountered difficulties in your work of strengthening Yiddishkeit in your community due to opponents and various concealments and obstructions—it is well known, based on what is explained in several sefarim, that one should not be impressed by this in the slightest. On the contrary, one must increase one’s courage in the war of Torah and mitzvos. And the adage of our Rebbes, the Nesi'im, is well known: "There is a strength in *ta'amulah* (outreach/propaganda) that it does not return empty."

We are currently in the *ikvesa d'Meshicha* (the footsteps of Moshiach), regarding which our Sages of blessed memory informed us that "there is no day that does not..." [have its own curse]. Surely, the intent of this notification was not to strike fear, but rather to inform us in advance so that the heart of a Yid would not fail him upon seeing the situation. Moreover, it is to add blessing to counter the curse, and not to be satisfied with the achievements of the day. For "G-d has made one thing opposite the other," and when the curse increases, Heaven forbid, there must be a corresponding increase and expansion of blessing and goodness—which are the matters of our Torah and mitzvos. "The advantage of light is from the darkness," and there is nothing that can stand before the will.

With respect and a blessing for success in your holy work.

## 596

B"H, 25 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 11 Nissan: It gave me pleasure to read that you have increased your fixed times for studying the teachings of Chassidus. Surely you do not satisfy yourself only with the hours you study with so-and-so, but you also review the concepts on your own. Most importantly, you contemplate their content in order to draw conclusions regarding practical application in your daily life. As the Chassidic adage goes, the hour of the day spent engaging in the study of Chassidus must permeate all the other hours of the twenty-four-hour cycle, and as the Mishnah states, "The deed is the essential thing."

Regarding what you write—that when one grasps the total nothingness of man and the vanity of his thoughts, it is difficult to escape depression:

The inner interpretation of the Mishnah, "The reward of a mitzvah is a mitzvah," is known. As explained in the teachings of Chassidus, the word *mitzvah* also implies *tzavta*—a bond and connection with the One who commands the mitzvah. This connection with the King of Kings, the Holy One, blessed be He, transforms the aforementioned "nothingness" into the true *yesh*, as it were. At every moment, one can connect and bond, since the possibility to serve Hashem exists in all matters. Indeed, our Sages said, "Let every soul praise Yah"—meaning that for every single breath, one must praise the Holy One, blessed be He. That is to say, it is within a person’s power to make even his breathing—though it may seemingly be a necessity—an act of service to Him, may He be blessed. See also Tanya, Chapter 31 (page 40b), "His soul should be precious in his eyes..." so as not to disturb or confuse the joy of the soul, etc.

Regarding what you write—that it seems to you that when you are invited to gatherings and the like, it is in your capacity as a journalist and not as a Jew seeking to draw closer, etc.:

On the face of it, it is difficult to understand what difference this makes to you. Since you know yourself that you are participating not only as someone seeking to draw closer, but also as someone who has already drawn closer, the intention of the one who invited you cannot change reality. On the other hand, if you feel that you are participating only as a journalist (and I hope this feeling does not correspond to reality at all), then it depends on you to change that. This change can be made in a single hour and in a single moment. Furthermore, in our times, when the necessity of the hour is to spread the wellsprings outward, all those who participate in *farbrengens* and gatherings—and who can subsequently bring the sparks of light and warmth from these gatherings to a circle that did not participate—it is their privilege and their holy duty. If the participant is a journalist, even if his participation is as a colleague in the profession, it is his duty and his privilege to utilize the opportunity to bring the matter to the readers of the newspaper, many of whom surely would not have known about the matter had they not read about it in this way, and so on.

Surely you utilized the days of Pesach to spread Yiddishkeit and spread the wellsprings to the full extent of your capabilities, and in doing so, to also draw close that son who is not explicitly mentioned in the Haggadah—that is, the one who is not present at the Seder at all. This is as explained in my general letter for this festival of Pesach, which I am sure you have read. I would be interested to hear of your activities in this regard.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above.

## 5,397

B"H, 25 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

I acknowledge receipt of your letter from the 2nd of Omer, along with the enclosure. The *Fast* newspaper issue published for the holiday of Pesach was also received. It is understood that your request that the enclosed material not be published will be honored.

Incidentally, in your letter you mention [name omitted]... It is surely unnecessary to remind you that in several of his books he fights against the proponents of Chassidus and much more, and in several matters, even against faith. Regarding the statement in the *Mechilta*, he acts not only against Moshe, the servant of Hashem, but also, *r"l*, against Hashem and His Torah. Even in those chapters that appear on the face of it to be neutral, they are liable to confuse the mind in general, and the youth of the United States in particular, regarding matters of opinion—to the point where who knows if it will be possible, or even within one's ability, to set them straight on the truth afterward. My words have already been said on another occasion: that in the United States, and in the Western Hemisphere in general, there prevails *am ha'aratzus*—meaning, a total lack of knowledge—unlike the situation in Europe in the past, where in secular circles there were [at least] false opinions. Therefore, this is not the way to utilize debates—that is, to also mention a corrupt opinion and then prove how false and deceitful it is. There is no need for this; on the contrary, it causes great harm and confusion. *Kal v'chomer*.

At a time of favor, I will mention all those you write about at the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, *ztzuklalah"h, nbg"m, zy"a*. May it be Hashem's will that you bring good news regarding them. It is surely unnecessary to emphasize that since they have turned to you with requests, you must explain to them that as they increase in Torah and *mitzvos*—for the Torah is a "Torah of life," and regarding its *mitzvos* it is said, "you shall live by them"—there will be a corresponding increase in the fulfillment of their hearts' desires in the aforementioned matters.

With blessings for good tidings.

## 598

B"H, 25 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 20 Nissan and those preceding it.

It is astonishing to hear about the *anash* activists you mention, who criticize everything being done in the vineyard of Chabad in the Holy Land—may it be rebuilt and established—when they have learned the method of sitting with folded arms. Even though they assess that if the work were done by them, it would be of the highest order, the Sages have already stated that anyone who has the ability to protest—and this does not necessarily mean protesting against something undesirable, but rather, anyone who has the ability to act and does not do so—[is held accountable]. This is especially true regarding any Chabad endeavor, as each one brings some benefit in *hafatzas hamaayanos chutzah*. This is a literal *mitzvah*, a clear *mitzvah* from the Torah, and not merely like the general *mitzvah* of *talmud torah*—which is itself equivalent to all others. Rather, through *hafatzas hamaayanos*, one strengthens and adds vitality to the fulfillment of all the *mitzvos asei* of the person to whom these *maayanos* reach, and prevents him from many deeds that should not be done. If they did not know about the *maor* that is in the Torah—which is the *pnimiyus hatorah* that has been revealed in our generation as *Chassidus*—they would take them lightly, and, G-d forbid, there are even those who would stumble in them, *rachmana litzlan*, and *kal v'chomer*...

With blessings for good tidings in all the aforementioned.

## 500

B"H, 27 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter, in which you write that you are troubled by distracting thoughts, etc.

I have already written many times to various members of anash that, although it is a simple and well-known matter, the exaggeration of the confusion caused by these distracting thoughts is also one of the enticements of the yetzer hara. It strives in every way and with every tactic to distract a person from the avodah of Hashem with joy and a glad heart. From this, it is understood that the less one exaggerates the matter—and furthermore, by means of complete diversion of attention (rather than through a direct battle with the distracting thoughts)—the situation will progressively improve. Since the mind is always wandering (as is explained in the maamarim of the festival of Pesach, that this is why thought is called a "river"), the rejection of these thoughts is accomplished by thinking about a different subject. It is understood that when that subject is Torah and its mitzvos, a little light pushes away much darkness, and all the more so when it is a great amount of light.

Among the matters and segulos to hasten the nullification of the aforementioned confusion are: studying Torah from the inside (from the text) in purity, and giving tzedakah before the morning davening on weekdays. Since, according to your letter, you have seen improvement in your other affairs, this should also prove to you clearly that the situation regarding the distracting thoughts has also improved, but the "opposing side" is concealing this improvement, etc.

With blessings for good tidings in all the aforementioned,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

P.S.
I trust you participated in the baking of matzah shemurah and, in general, in the activities of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad in Jerusalem, the Holy City, may it be rebuilt and established speedily.

## 511

B"H, 28 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

In response to your letter of the 23rd [of Nissan]—I am surprised by the gap in your writing until now, but we do not cry over the past, and may it be Hashem’s will that from here on in, this conduct will improve.

In it, you write about your teaching with the students. Surely, even though you do not mention it, you are seeking ways and methods to instill in them a Chassidic spirit and *yiras shamayim*. This is accomplished not only through study, but also through conversation regarding these matters, and through the appropriate conduct of a teacher, serving as a living example of behavior befitting a Chossid and one who possesses *yiras shamayim*. There is nothing that can stand before the will, especially when the merit of the many—the students and those you influence—assists you.

With blessings.

## 512

B"H, 28 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and distinguished individual, who engages in communal needs,
Rabbi Tzion Rachamim, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

...And since Divine Providence has placed you in a position where you can exert influence in strengthening and disseminating Yiddishkeit—thereby bringing the hearts of the children of Yisrael closer to our Father in Heaven—and knowing the saying of the Baal Shem Tov, which we have heard many times from my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, that the affection Hashem holds for every single Jew is like the affection for an only son born to his parents in their old age, and even infinitely more than that, it is understood that the reward for a person who brings about this closeness is beyond expression. This is the channel and the vessel to increase the blessings of Hashem that are needed. Although it is also understood that the verse "and do not hide yourself from your own flesh" applies to him as well, to increase his study of the holy Torah, both in the revealed aspects and in Chassidus, from time to time; and there is nothing that can stand in the way of will.

You are surely aware of the custom of our predecessors to recite the monthly Tehillim portion every day after the morning prayers—as the Tehillim is divided according to the days of the month—and in any case, you should maintain this from now on, and endeavor to implement this practice wherever your influence reaches.

With blessing.

## 503

B"H, 28 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, and meticulous in his work, a man of many accomplishments, R' Shlomo, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter from the 23rd of Nissan, along with its enclosure. I was pleased to read that you gathered during the days of the festival of Pesach, and that all the teachers also assembled before Pesach for a fitting farbrengen. Surely, the success of all this will awaken in them additional strength for the future, especially according to what is explained in Chassidus: that the avodah of Hashem in the summer is easier than in the winter days. This is particularly true during the days that mark the beginning of our freedom and the receiving of our Torah on the festival of Shavuos, when every single day has a special mitzvah within it—Sefiras HaOmer. Furthermore, the mitzvah of the previous day remains present even in the day that follows; this is why the phrasing is "two days to the Omer" and not "day two." Every preceding mitzvah adds strength to the fulfillment of all the other 612 mitzvos, as the Mishnah states, "One mitzvah leads to another."

Regarding what you wrote about the matter of tefillin—you must explain that this is not merely a matter of extra beauty (hiddur), but rather a distinction between tefillin that are kosher and those that are the opposite. In the vast majority of cases, one can recognize this even through an external inspection—that is, by checking the parshiyos and the batim—and all the more so if one had the ability to inspect the interior, i.e., the kashrus of the sofer, and the rest is understood.

Surely, if there is a possibility to translate my general letters for the festival of Pesach into the local language (provided it is a good translation—that is, one that is appropriate to the subject matter and in a style that will make a good impression on the readers, including the style and grammar of the language into which it is being translated, etc.), it is worthwhile to make an effort in this, even if it entails expenses. It is understood that all of the above applies also to the other general letters, provided you find a good translator.

Perhaps there is value in compiling a booklet of the general letters, in which one would find at least one letter for every festival and holiday throughout the year. Surely you will contemplate this in accordance with the conditions of your location and the residents, and may it be the will of Hashem that you succeed in this as well.

With blessings and regards to all his colleagues in the holy work of fulfilling His will, may He be blessed, to make for Him a dwelling place in the lower worlds.

**M. Schneerson**

## 504

B"H, 28 Nissan, 5717, Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and scholarly, who engages in communal needs, R' Dovid, may he live:

Peace and blessing!

Following a very long hiatus, your two letters were received, along with the enclosures and the *pidyon nefesh* which will be read at an auspicious time at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory. It is surprising that there has been such a silence in your correspondence until now, and also that you omitted mentioning your *kvi'us itim* (fixed times) for the study of Chassidus, both for yourself and for others.

Regarding your question as to which language you should use to study with the *bochurim* and students:

Since they must be informed of the *mitzvos* of Hashem and those things which must not be done in practice, it is understood that there is no time to wait in these studies until they understand a language other than the one to which they are already accustomed. Therefore, you must teach them in the language they currently understand, and utilize all the time at your disposal to influence them—not to teach a language, but to instill in them a spirit of *yiras shamayim*, love of Hashem, and love of the Torah, and this is understood.

Regarding your question about the *tzedakah* of *ShP"H*—I have enclosed a copy of the *sicha* in which this matter is explained.

Regarding your question as to my opinion on what name to give the *beis medrash* or the group—it is better to have a name that does not restrict them (*nit binden*), and therefore, I do not see the logic in using a name that is already used by another organization.

May Hashem grant you the merit and success to increase your study for yourself and your study with others, both in *nigleh* and in Chassidus and its guidance. May you take part in the inner mission of our current era: *hafatzas hama'ayanos chutzah* (spreading the wellsprings outward), which, as the Baal Shem Tov states in his well-known *Iggeres HaKodesh*, is the preparation and the vessel for the coming of Moshiach Tzidkeinu, immediately.

With the blessing that you and your wife, may she live, raise your children, may they live, for Torah, *chuppah*, and good deeds.

## 505

B"H, 29 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn, N.Y.

To all the members and congregants of the Anash Lubavitch Shul of Boro Park, Brooklyn,

Peace and Blessing!

It was with pleasure and joy that I received the good news that, in a good and auspicious hour, a Rav has been chosen, and the letter of appointment has been presented to the esteemed Rabbi, the Chassid, G-d-fearing and learned, involved in communal needs, a man of fine character, etc., Rabbi Yitzchak Dov Ushpal, may he live and be well. Therefore, it requires, as a leader and Rav, a man of spirit capable of vigorous action so that they may be able to elevate the shul to the necessary height.

Since surely several shiurim in Nigleh and Chassidus have been and will be established in this shul—

His promise is already given to bless each of those who take part in all of the above, with whatever they and their families, may they live and be well, require, both in material and spiritual matters.

With respect and blessing for all that is good.

## 510

B"H, 29 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, and industrious, R' Tzvi, may he live:

Peace and blessing!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter from Erev Shabbos. Being at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, today, I mentioned you, your wife, and your children, may they live.

Regarding what you wrote concerning your neighborhood emptying out, etc.—one should look into how others have handled similar situations in the past. Perhaps there is room to open a shul, even on a small scale (*b'ze'er anpin*), in the neighborhood to which people are moving. The shul could be named a "branch" of your current shul. The benefit would be that the *baalei batim* would not drift apart or become affiliated with a different shul. With blessing.
