## 507

29 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter—in which you did not note the date of writing—where you outline the story of your life and what you have been through, and mention that you are now engaged in the field of education, striving to instill in your students yiras shamayim, knowledge of Torah, and the performance of mitzvos, etc., but that your spirit has fallen because you see that when they leave your domain, "the wind has carried away your labor," etc.

Regarding this in general, it is difficult for a person to gauge whether the wind has indeed carried away their labor. On the contrary, it is highly probable that an education spanning years in the spirit of Yiddishkeit must leave a lasting impression. Even if you see them behaving in a manner that is not as it should be or as is desired, a person only sees what is before their eyes—the external conduct and the behavior at that specific moment. We do not know how they conduct themselves at home in essential matters, nor how they would have behaved in their affairs had they not been under the influence of the spirit of Yiddishkeit for months and years. It is simple, and the adage of our Rebbeim is well-known: there is a *chazakah* (presumption) that effort expended in *taamulah* (outreach) does not return empty, even regarding adults, and certainly regarding children and youth. It is also understood and simple that in order to be "one who moistens so that others may be moistened," a greater measure of yiras shamayim is required. But surely this, too, is in his own hands; if one truly desires, there is always room to add in matters of taharah and kedushah, since this is connected to the Creator, who is Ein Sof. Regardless of the level of perfection a person may reach, there remains an infinite distance between him and the ultimate perfection, which is infinite, as are all the attributes and qualities of the Creator, blessed be He.

Regarding what you write about being troubled by thoughts and contemplations that distract you during your davening: according to what is explained in the seforim, and particularly in the books of Chassidus and mussar, these thoughts mostly stem from undesirable actions in the past. Therefore, along with the advice given for distracting thoughts—which is to divert one’s attention from them by anchoring the mind in matters of Torah, mitzvos, and taharah in general (which also rules out the approach of fighting these distracting thoughts by proving to oneself how undesirable and disgraceful they are, for that is not the concept of diverting one's attention, as is understood)—one must also erase the undesirable impression left by the past. In addition to complete teshuvah for the past and a firm resolution for the future, there must also be the concept of "weight and distance" in the same area where the undesirable and disgraceful action occurred. For example, if there was a deficiency in "doing good" or an act contrary to "turning from evil" regarding a specific mitzvah, one must add *hiddur* (beautification) in that area from now on. If there were obstacles involved in a mitzvah that is no longer relevant to the one who stumbled, one should influence those who are still involved in it so that they add *hiddur* in the performance of that specific mitzvah. Since all of Yisrael are responsible for one another, this constitutes teshuvah and uproots the impression of the undesirable matter.

In general, for davening to be more readily accepted, it is proper to set aside a few coins for tzedakah before davening every weekday. You should also strive to daven in a state of bodily purity by immersing in a mikveh when necessary, and to daven in a place where the tzibbur davens, for the atmosphere there is more refined.

Regarding what you write about your son and daughter, may they live, you surely do not rely solely on your own words to them, but strive for them to meet people who can influence them in matters of Torah and mitzvos. We see tangibly that sometimes children are more easily influenced by strangers than by their parents, because regarding strangers, it does not compromise their sense of independence. Furthermore, a person who is not a close relative can speak without the added emotional charge that is sometimes detrimental, whereas parents and relatives do not always control their spirit.

You surely know of the three daily portions—Chumash, Tehillim, and Tanya—that are appropriate for every soul, and you should keep them, at the very least, from now on.

With blessings.

## 508

508

By the Grace of Hashem, 29 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

I have received the visitor who is requesting an answer to his question. I assume the intent is the question of whether it is permitted to take semen for medical examination, in order to determine the appropriate treatment for the birth of healthy, viable children.

Generally, it is not my practice to issue halachic rulings, especially in matters such as these. I have made an exception in this instance due to an instruction from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, to whom someone once posed a question regarding the examination of semen in general, as requested by a doctor as mentioned above. He answered him: "To have children, one must be prepared for mesiras nefesh." However one interprets these words, it is understood that the ruling is to proceed with the examination. In such matters, if people are stringent, it becomes not a matter of permission but of obligation—meaning, those who are stringent upon themselves—without a sufficiently strong foundation—not to provide the semen for examination in such a case, must bring proof. Although, because of the brazen ones of the generation, it is questionable whether this instruction should be publicized with trumpets and a loud blast.

Regarding the method of obtaining the semen, there are several types: 1) From the husband. 2) From the wife, by her placing a *moch* (absorbent material) in that place before the act of intimacy, and afterwards they bring the *moch* to the doctor. 3) That she does not place a *moch*, but rather that they take the semen from the womb after the act. According to what is brought in the Responsa of the Tzemach Tzedek to Shulchan Aruch Even HaEzer, end of chapter 7, it is understood that the third method is halachically preferable to the first method, and even to the second (as there is a line of reasoning that it is akin to "casting upon the trees," etc. In addition to the fact that many doctors are not satisfied when the semen is taken from the *moch*). Therefore, a telegram was sent to you, agreeing with the logic in the question posed in your letter—to take it for examination from the womb after the act of intimacy, in the natural manner.

It is known that there are *Acharonim* who are generally stringent regarding the aforementioned. Specifically, those from Hungary are stringent in this matter, as well as regarding the question in the Responsa of the Tzemach Tzedek mentioned there. But (in addition to the fact that generally in these days, when the level of *yiras shamayim* has diminished, a stringency in such a matter—whose goal is to add purity and prevent the prohibition of wasting seed—is liable to cause matters **opposite** to this intention, because the brazen ones have multiplied, may Hashem spare us, and therefore even among the Rabbis of Hungary in the **most** recent generations they have begun to be lenient in this, and in any case) we have nothing but the word of the King, who is our sages, our *Nasi*, the Tzemach Tzedek, from whose waters we drink and by whose light we walk.

Furthermore, it should be noted that there are those who tend toward the view that if it is necessary to permit such a thing, one should use a rubber sheath or the like. Those who lean toward this are indeed among the leading halachic authorities, etc. But in my humble opinion, and after reflection, this logic does not seem correct to me at all, since this is not the natural way of intimacy. I was pleased to find this opinion in the Responsa*.

With blessings that the Creator of the world and its Guide should bless you and your wife, *tichyeh*, with the fulfillment of your hearts' desires for healthy, viable children very soon, and in the best possible way, and that you may raise them to be *Chassidim*, *yirei shamayim*, and scholars.

Awaiting good tidings,

**M. Schneerson**

P.S. Regarding what you wrote at the end of your letter, that you have distress because so-and-so is angry at you for having entered, etc.—the demand of our *Rabbeinu HaZaken* in his *Iggeres HaKodesh* (end of section 22) is already known: "As water reflects the face to the face," etc.

And in this, it does not make much of a difference who begins the change for the good, as in any case, it will cause "as water reflects the face" from the other side as well.

***

*Printed in Likkutei Sichos Vol. 12, p. 180, and completed based on a photograph of the letter.*

*And he answered him: See also above, Vol. 13, letter 4557, and the references in the notes there.*

## 509

509

B"H, 29 Nissan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and accomplished man,
R' Avraham Mordechai Shmuel, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings!

After a pause, I have again received your two letters from after Pesach and the two preceding them. In them, you write briefly about the farbrengen, the conference of the Merkaz L'Inyonei Chinuch, and the audience with the Chief Rabbi of England. I believe I have already written to you that precisely where one should elaborate, people tend to be brief. In a farbrengen, and especially in a conference or an audience, the more one details, the better, not the opposite. Perhaps a more detailed letter regarding the aforementioned will still be received.

It is my hope that since you write that the cause of the negative aspects of the conference was a lack of proper preparation, that at the very least, from here on out, there will be interest in preparing for all Chabad matters well in advance and with the necessary attention. Success in such matters is not merely success at a specific hour or on a specific day; rather, the extent of the influence upon those participating, gathering, and assembling depends upon it. It is well understood, therefore, that every effort and endeavor is worthwhile since it concerns the many. After all the justifications, it is still completely incomprehensible that after being involved in such matters for so many years, and seeing clearly how much preparation and planning in advance matters, we still encounter this obstacle anew, time and again.

I was pleased to read in your letter that you are preparing to assist with the registration. I am confident, based on what was said above, that this will be done with an organized plan and the necessary vigor. This is a matter where time is of the essence; how much longer will the lack of preparation be a stumbling block for us?

It is understood that all of the above is not directed at you personally, and you have the permission and the right to convey this to all the activists among Anash. Anyone who contemplates our era can easily recognize the truth: that every person is an activist. Whoever does not act in practice is only following the advice of his yetzer hara. Furthermore, he is sinning by not utilizing the strengths and possibilities granted to him by Hashem, which, as it were, are going to waste. May it be His will that you bring good news of a fundamental change in all the aforementioned, very soon.

With blessings for good tidings, with proper health, peace of mind, and outstanding success in your activities, especially in spreading the wellsprings outward.

P.S.

Regarding your question as to whether, outside of your teaching position, you should also be involved in the affairs of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad: my perspective on this is also known—that everyone must take part in spreading the wellsprings outward. This is all the more true for the young, who have the strength for it and have been gifted with this by Divine Providence. However, naturally, one should not set hard and fast rules regarding the time to be dedicated to these activities, as it depends on the conditions of the place, the time, and one's intuition. In accordance with all of this, one decides for each and every activity [...] only to the extent that it does not interfere at all with your holy work in chinuch at this time...

## 510

[Nissan 5717]

Regarding what you wrote concerning the bris milah: It is understood that one must make every possible effort to ensure that *priah* is performed separately. The same applies to *metzitzah*—it is alarming that it is not being performed at all. At the very least, it should be done using a glass tube. It would be appropriate for you and R' Shlomo Matousov, *shye*, to study the *kuntres* on *metzitzah* in the *Sdei Chemed*. The *Sdei Chemed* is accepted by all the Sephardim in particular. Afterward, you should enter into a discussion and negotiation regarding this with the rabbis of Casablanca, etc.

I told them that they should endeavor to perform the *milah* separately and the *priah* separately, but regarding the *metzitzah*, they are unable to rectify it, for if they perform it by mouth, they will be removed from their positions.

## 511

511

B”H, Rosh Chodesh Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the Kfar Chabad Committee,
May Hashem protect them and grant them life.

Peace and Blessing!

During the conversations held here in recent weeks, several points have become clear to me. It is surprising that I had not heard of them until now. They are as follows:

1) Last year, when the village was being fenced with wire (which area was fenced? Was the old village included, or the other side as well?), I have just been told that after they began in the usual manner, the Mara D’asra, Rabbi R.S. shlit”a, suggested that it be done in a way that would be kosher for an Eiruv. The suggestion was received favorably, and it was done accordingly.

2) Over the past few years, there have been discussions with committee members or leaders of the village regarding preparations for the upcoming Shmittah year. Those who spoke to me feel that nothing has been done in this area, even though it is known throughout Eretz Yisrael, may it be rebuilt and established, that in Komemiyut and Chefetz Chaim they observe Shmittah according to Halacha, and they begin preparations for it immediately after the conclusion of the previous Shmittah. Although one does not cry over the past, at the very least, one must now take an interest in the aforementioned. Certainly, those involved in this in Komemiyut and Chefetz Chaim will provide them with all the necessary information.

3) I spoke here with the esteemed Chossid, R. A. Drizin, and I have also received letters from members of Kfar Chabad regarding the organization of groups (artel) for handicrafts, knitting, and the like. They explained to me that the obstacle here is the necessity for working capital in an amount that individuals are unable to secure or provide.

It is understood that this reasoning seemed surprising to me, as there are certainly several banks that provide loans for such matters, though the matter was not absolutely clear to me. However, last week it was stated to me clearly that the Bank HaPoalim and similar institutions do provide loans for such purposes, provided, of course, that it is proven to them that the loan will be used for the stated purpose and that the individuals are capable, to a certain extent at least, of the craft and work in which they wish to engage.

It is possible that the village committee’s guarantee will also be required for this, but that is certainly a feasible matter. Furthermore, it should be added that the organization of such groups would also be beneficial for point (2) above, regarding the Shmittah year, as is understood.

It is surprising that, until now, no detailed information has arrived regarding the reception, the gathering, etc. It is possible that this has become a case of "a pot belonging to partners," where each relies on the other. I have already said that in such matters, it is better to have double the effort than to have it be completely lacking.

With blessings for good tidings regarding all the above, and for success in all the village’s affairs, both general and specific.

## 512

B"H, Rosh Chodesh Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter in which you write regarding the issue currently before you, which is connected to the laws and statutes of the state—in the details of which there is also a question of *daas Torah*—it is not always possible to clarify all the necessary details in writing.

Therefore, after you clarify the laws of the state regarding the aforementioned matter, you should turn to an observant rabbi in your vicinity and ask him for a ruling according to our holy Torah, the *Torah* of life.

Since everything is by *hashgacha pratis*, and you are writing to me with this question—which, as mentioned above, should be addressed to a local rabbi—there is surely an additional dimension to this. It is highly likely that this is an awakening to increase in *Torah* and *mitzvos*. You are surely familiar with at least the main points of the teachings of *Chassidus*, its guidance, and its customs, given that you know my address. Therefore, I hope that from now on you will at least set fixed times for the study of this *Torah* and increase your involvement in matters of *Chassidus*, including those that pertain to the fulfillment of *mitzvos* and the conduct of a person in general. This is in accordance with the commandment, "In all your ways know Him," which the *Rambam* discusses at length in *Shemonah Perakim*, Chapter 5, and which is brought as *halacha* in the *Tur* and *Shulchan Aruch*, *Orach Chaim*, section 231; see there. Indeed, through strengthening oneself in *Torah* and its *mitzvos*, one increases the blessings of *Hashem* in all that a person and his household require.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 513

B”H, Rosh Chodesh Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

After a long interval, your letter was received—without an indication of when it was written—in which you suggest traveling overseas regarding matters of shidduchim, etc.

Now, in my opinion—and I believe I have already told you this explicitly—the obstacle to shidduchim is that you are overly selective regarding matters that are not relevant at all. Furthermore, there is the fear of making a decision and finalizing it, because after that, the matter is settled. Based on this, it is not understood how it would help if you were in a different country. If you had decided once and for all that there is no perfection in the world among women, just as there is no perfection among men—for only the Creator is perfect—and therefore one must focus on the essential matters and pay no attention to the trivialities of the trivial, you would have already finalized a shidduch long ago. And even though you are correct in your claim that your current location is not a suitable place to find one, you have been here for several weeks...

And you have not decided, and in my opinion, it is because of the aforementioned reasons.

Regarding the practical aspect: if you decide to travel overseas, this will immediately cause you to lose focus on all the suggestions in the United States, which is obviously the complete opposite of the intended goal. Since the vacation days are approaching, they should be utilized as necessary—meaning, the preparations and inquiries that can be done by others should be done **now**, through parents, relatives, friends, etc. And when you come here... you will decide accordingly, based on the above. May Hashem guide you in what is truly good for you, in both material and spiritual matters.

It is understood that all of the above concerns the ways of nature, but first and foremost, there must be an increase in matters of Torah and mitzvos, for ultimately, the verse states, "And from Hashem comes an intelligent wife."

With blessings for success in the above.

## 1414

B"H, Rosh Chodesh Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter: once you write your name and your mother’s name, you will be mentioned at the holy resting place of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, for a good match and for success in general.

Regarding what you write about being "heavy of speech" (a stutter): engrave in your mind the concept that Hashem, may He be blessed and exalted, oversees every single person with *hashgacha pratis* (Divine providence), and that Hashem is the essence of good. Therefore, there is no reason to fear any person. When you are strong in this faith, you will naturally not become agitated when you wish to begin speaking before others, and you will speak calmly. Then, little by little, your articulation will improve. In any case, this does not hinder you from finding a good match. You should involve yourself in this through natural means as well; through friends and acquaintances, look for someone whose primary concern is *yiras Hashem* (fear of Heaven), without focusing on trivial, secondary matters. If you do this, you will succeed in the aforementioned.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 515

515

B”H, 1 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn

To the esteemed Chossid, man of many accomplishments, engaged in communal needs, R’ Menachem Zev, may he live, Halevi

Shalom u’Bracha!

In response to your letter, which was slightly delayed in transit, in which you write regarding Mr. ... whose livelihood situation is not as it should be, and who also suffered a theft, etc.

At a time of favor, I will mention him and his family, may they live, at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, in accordance with the aforementioned content.

You should certainly explain to him the well-known statement of our Sages, “Give tithes so that you may become wealthy.” In a situation where one is not satisfied with their livelihood, one must increase the tzedakah that they are accustomed to setting aside, even if it was previously as much as required—or in the words of our Sages, as much as one ought to do. And certainly, if there was room even before this to increase the tzedakah contribution, one should do so now. This should be done specifically with joy and a good heart, trusting in the Creator of the world to keep His promise: “Test Me now in this... and I will pour out for you a blessing until there is no more” (Malachi 3:10).

Regarding the incident of the theft, the Iggeres HaKodesh of the Alter Rebbe, his son the Mitteler Rebbe, and his grandson the Tzemach Tzedek is already well known: that after an incident of loss, the attribute of mercy—which is even greater than the attribute of Chesed—is bestowed without limit, etc. However, for every influence and blessing from Above, one must create appropriate and expansive vessels, and these vessels are the matters of Torah and mitzvos performed with hiddur and joy.

It would be appropriate, since you will be mentioning the aforementioned individual and his family at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, that he establish a regular study of Chabad Chassidus—to which my father-in-law, the Rebbe, dedicated his life—on Shabbos, and on Mondays and Thursdays, even if only for a short time, especially on Shabbos. It is also good to observe the Rebbe’s takkanah of reciting the monthly Tehillim—as the Tehillim is divided for the days of the month—after Shacharis. It is understood that all of the above should be done without a neder.

Surely the wife of the aforementioned individual follows the custom of the righteous daughters of Israel to set aside tzedakah before lighting candles every Erev Shabbos and Erev Yom Tov; at the very least, she should conduct herself in this manner from now on.

With blessings for good tidings regarding all of the above.

Since this is not the first time, the impression of the first time surely still remains, for holiness never departs from its place, especially when the one providing the inspiration comes into contact [with them] at various times regarding matters of hisorerus. I am writing regarding the above to emphasize the fulfillment of the promise of our holy Rebbes that effort [in outreach] does not return empty, which adds strength to such efforts moving forward.

With blessings for good news in all the above.

## 1818

B"H, 2 Iyar 5717
Brooklyn.

For the commandment of the Ribono shel Olam is to "ascend in holiness," and the body of a Yid is holy. With blessings for a refuah and for good tidings.

519

B"H, 2 Iyar, 5717

## 520

B"H, 2 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the avreich Yehoshafat, may he live,

Shalom u'Bracha!

It was a pleasure to receive your letter from Motzaei Shabbos, in which you write the details of your travels and visits in the vicinity of Yerushalayim, may it be rebuilt and established, during the days of the Pesach holiday, as well as before and after. Surely, this was done in an organized and focused manner, for we are in the final days of the galus, and therefore every moment is most precious, and every active power and every opportunity must be utilized **as much as** possible. For this reason, one must guard against doubling efforts while failing to utilize an opportunity to the extent necessary (although, of course, doubling is better than a deficiency).

It is appropriate that, at the very least, the main points of the aforementioned speeches be recorded in writing and discussed at a meeting of all the travelers and visitors, for "in the multitude of counselors there is safety," should the matters require improvement, supplementation, or correction. Those that are perfectly suitable should be brought to the attention of all the travelers so that they, too, may utilize them in the appropriate places.

Regarding what you wrote that in some places you visited without prior notice:

While if it is impossible otherwise, or in the case of special circumstances, one must act as described, in general, this is not organized avodah. It can also cause unpleasantness and difficulty in influencing the place due to the lack of notification, and this is easy to understand.

Regarding what you wrote about studying Da"Ch there, or reviewing a maamar, and the like: this question, too, must be raised at the meeting of the travelers and visitors, with the participation of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad as well as the elders of Anash, since it is a fundamental question and requires discussion with the appropriate gravity.

With blessings for good tidings.

## 522

B"H, 2 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter from before the holiday of Pesach, you will surely endeavor to fulfill your heart’s desires for the good by increasing your *kvius itim* (fixed times) for the study of *Chassidus*—the *pnimiyus haTorah* (inner dimension of Torah), the "light of the Torah." If this is an urgent necessity for everyone, it is all the more so for a descendant of our holy *Rebbeim Nesi’einu*, the family of the *Beis HaRav*. It is understood that regarding the *hafatzas hamaayanos* (spreading the wellsprings) in every place you can reach, your obligation is many times greater than that of other *anash*, and *kal v’chomer* (it follows a fortiori). Since you have this obligation and privilege, you have certainly been granted the strengths to fulfill your unique duty.

Regarding your question about your future arrangements, whether in farming... and what you wrote—that such an arrangement contradicts the possibility of Torah study—it is understood that this is not the case. Farmers and those who manage estates are also obligated in Torah study, and *Hakadosh Baruch Hu* does not make unreasonable demands, etc. This is especially true when we see tangibly how many farmers and agriculturalists engage in Torah study and succeed. Furthermore, in addition to all other reasons, it is precisely in our times that we need to increase the number of Torah students who work in these professions, in order to uproot the false notion that those who wish to work in agriculture, etc., are thereby exempt from various matters of Torah and *mitzvos*. It is understood that a living example of this is more effective than many words of propaganda and inspiration, and the *zechus harabim* (merit of the many) assists the one who engages in this. As mentioned, this is in addition to other reasons, including those you wrote in your letter.

It is possible that you will say that your attempt—to study Torah while also continuing with the farm—is too difficult. However, the adage of our holy *Rebbeim Nesi’einu* is already known: just as a person is obligated to know the measure of his shortcomings, he is equally obligated not to diminish his strengths. For this, too, pertains to the *avodas Hashem* as required, and the minimization of one’s own strengths is often one of the stratagems of the *Yetzer Hara* to prevent a person from fulfilling the task for which he was created: to serve his Creator, and *kal v’chomer*.

With blessings.

## 523

B"H, 2 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter from Erev Shabbos Kodesh, thank you for the good news regarding the improvement in the health of your daughter, tichyeh. May it be the will of Hashem that this be a good beginning for further good news in this matter and in all other areas, especially in accordance with the well-known statement of our Sages that whenever one is granted a benefit, it is a sign of long life (Bava Basra 12b).

Regarding what you wrote—that you needed to exert much effort and assistance to ensure that a certain individual would establish a time for learning Chassidus, and you also mention the reasons [he gave for his reluctance], one of which is the claim that Hashem is satisfied with only 250 people in His world, etc., and that they do not care, etc.

It is astonishing that their own mouths have caused them to stumble by specifically mentioning this number, and the wise will understand. It is obvious and simple that this argument is the opposite of the **foundations** of our Torah, the Torah of kindness, especially in this era of ours, in accordance with what is explained by our Sages in many places regarding the verse, "As in the days of your exodus from the land of Egypt, I will show you wonders." This means that the future Redemption and its aspects are parallel to the days of the exodus from Egypt and their aspects. This also applies to the teaching of our Sages regarding those days, which is likewise applicable to the present time (Mechilta, Yisro; Devarim Rabbah, Parshas 7:8): "If even one person from Israel were missing, the Shechinah would not have been revealed to them." And the revelation of Mattan Torah was wondrous even for the level of Moshe Rabbeinu, and the author of that statement does not distinguish regarding any single person of Israel—the people of the tribe of Dan were also included, and the wise will understand. In general, such cruelty, and the exclusion of 250 people from among the six hundred thousand of Israel, forces one to wonder about the true character of the one who says such things.

May it be the will of Hashem that, in His kindness and mercy, He will pour out a spirit from on high upon them, and that they will return in time from words that separate between the children of Israel and our Father in Heaven. There is no need to elaborate on such a shocking matter; it is surprising that you even engaged in discussion with such people, and certainly, any debate or negotiation with them is out of the question. My hope is that, at the very least, not only will they not weaken your energy in spreading the wellsprings of Chassidus, but that you will increase your courage in this. The words of the Arizal and his student, Rav Chaim Vital, are well known and founded on pillars of gold: that delaying the study of the inner dimension of the Torah delays the Redemption, Heaven forbid, and prolongs the bitter exile of the Shechinah and the children of Israel, etc. See the shocking words that make one’s hair stand on end in the introduction of Rav Chaim Vital to Shaar HaHakdamos—and who can come after the King?

As you requested, I will mention all those you wrote about at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory.

With blessings for good news in all that was mentioned.

## 5424

B"H, 4 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and distinguished individual, engaged in communal needs,
R' Shmuel Yosef, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

In response to your letter from before the festival of Pesach, which took a long time to arrive, in which you write about the results of your activities, etc.

It is surprising that there is any back-and-forth on this matter, after the assurance of our Rebbeim, the Nesi'im, that there is a strength to *ta'amulah* (outreach) that never returns empty. Even though it is understood that the results can be "good," "even better," or "very good," in any case, there is no room for disappointment or discouragement, even if they are "no more than good" and "not yet better." It is certainly not the place for back-and-forth regarding whether to engage in activities, or G-d forbid, to stop.

Regarding your question as to whether you should pressure a certain individual concerning matters of *mitzvos*, or if it is possible that because of this he will become frightened—especially since the matter at hand does not concern basic *kashrus*, but rather an addition to what is merely *kasher*—it is understood that one cannot set hard and fast rules, as it depends on the soul-nature of the person being approached.

One can learn something about this from the halachic ruling regarding which matters we say it is "better that they be unintentional sinners" and which matters we do not, and from the other side, the command "you shall surely rebuke" (even a hundred times, and even to the point of a reprimand, etc.), as per the well-known instructions of our Sages.

As you requested, I will mention you, as well as Mr. Chaim Menush, may he live, at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, for whatever they need in general, and for preparation for the festival of Shavuos and for the receiving of the Torah with joy and inwardness.

Especially since we are currently in the days of *Sefirah*, where from day to day light is added. This is why the phrasing of the *Sefirah* is "today two days," and not "the second day," which is how it would seemingly need to be said. This is because the light drawn down on the first day does not depart, but rather exists also on the second day, and the second day adds to it. And so it goes until the completion of the *Sefirah*, with light constantly being added, until we hear—and are hearing—the voice going out from the four directions, above and below: "I am Hashem your G-d," etc.

With blessings for good tidings.

## 5,125

5,125

B"H, 4 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and distinguished individual, occupied with communal needs, R' A. Sh., may he live.

Peace and blessing!

After a hiatus, I was pleased to receive your letter from after Pesach, and I am especially pleased to read in it that you participated in the *kinus* in Kfar Chabad and experienced spiritual closeness. May Hashem help that the intention of every *kinus* and *hisva'adus* be fulfilled in this gathering as well: to bring the *ma'ayanos* of *Toras HaChassidus*, its guidance, and its *minhagim* to ever-increasing and widening circles, in such a way that the listeners and participants become *tofe'ach al menas lehatpi'ach*—moistening others—to all those with whom they come into contact; and there is nothing that can stand before the will.

Especially as we find ourselves in the days of *Sefirah*, where from day to day more light is added—a light that is connected to the receiving of the Torah with joy and inwardness. This is indeed the explanation for why, in the *nusach* of *Sefirah*, we say "two days to the *Omer*" rather than "the second day," because the light of yesterday has not departed; it is present today as well, with the addition of today's light of *Sefirah*. Thus, it accumulates from day to day until one hears the Divine call, "I am Hashem your G-d," from the four directions, from above and below, as related in the *Midrash*.

With blessings for good tidings in all the aforementioned.

## 5,426

B"H, 4 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 26 Nissan, thank you for the good news regarding the organization of the committee for the benefit of the yeshiva’s affairs, as well as the initial preparations for the annual dinner. In connection with this, and regarding your question in your previous letter concerning the *nigunim*, the practice here is to avoid playing any *nigunim* that could lead to differences of opinion or conflict. If a particular *nigun* causes or draws in others that lead to such differences, then that *nigun* is not played either. The explanation for this is that the purpose of the dinner and the like is not to take part in this dispute on either side; rather, there is only one goal for the dinner: to establish the status of an institution that provides benefit to all circles. Consequently, all circles are obligated to assist it and support it, and no one who participates in strengthening the institution should be alienated.

It is understood and simple that the best approach is to avoid placing this question on the agenda altogether (to postpone the discussion on this from time to time, etc.), because even the aforementioned response is not accepted by everyone in its true sense, as desire misleads their intellect, and *kal v'chomer* [it is easy to understand].

With blessings for outstanding success in your holy work and for good news.

#### ***5,426***

**The nigunim:** See also above, Vol. 12, letter 4,013.

## 527

527

B”H, 4 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, learned, and diligent scholar,
Rabbi Avraham Yitzchak, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter, in which you write regarding the sending of the two books you published, which were received.

Thank you for your thoughtfulness in sending them; I am certain you will continue this good practice in the future.

As per your request, publications from Kehot will be sent to you. The second volume of the memoirs of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, is not currently available.

Regarding the *avreich* Menachem, may he live, Halevi Hilbitz—greetings. I am certain he will also write to you himself.

I will take this opportunity to express my appreciation for your publication of *Toldos Nesi’ei Toras HaChassidus*. Our current era is especially in need of such books. Because of the confusion of opinions and the malevolent winds that do not refrain from falsifying the truth, it is necessary to emphasize, again and again, the simple and well-known facts—even to our own generation—regarding who the great leaders of Israel and its *Nesi’im* are. It is by their light that the Jewish people walk in all their affairs, and it is this light that has sustained them to this very day, despite all the tempests and waves that have passed over them.

I have already spoken elsewhere regarding the intent of our Sages’ statement concerning the generation of the footsteps of Moshiach: "There is no day whose curse is not greater than the day before," etc. It is obvious that our Sages, who loved Israel, did not intend, G-d forbid, to instill fear and dread without purpose. The purpose of this notification is twofold:

1) When days of double and redoubled darkness arrive, to the point that the situation is as described—that "there is no day," etc.—the heart of a Jewish person should not fail him. He knows full well that these are the birth pangs of Moshiach, etc.

2) When there is an increase in the "other side" (*sitra achra*), it is understood and simple that there must be a corresponding increase in the side of holiness and blessing. The logic and argument that "it is sufficient to remain in the current state of goodness in the side of holiness, as we stood yesterday and the day before," is refuted. Since there has been an increase in the "other side," one cannot stand in the battle except by increasing the side of goodness.

This is also the explanation for the phenomenon where, from generation to generation, there is an increase in the revelation of the holy Torah. As our Sages said, "Everything that a veteran student will eventually innovate was already given to Moshe at Sinai"—it is revealed generation after generation, in addition to the Torah that existed until then, even though, on the face of it, the earlier generations were more spiritually refined.

In this, there is also a refutation of the well-known argument: "If the study of Chassidus is so necessary for Torah and mitzvos to be as they should be—with light and vitality—why was it not revealed in its fullness in previous generations?"

This is similar to the well-known question regarding many matters of Torah and mitzvos, where people do not want to accept the rulings of the later authorities (*Acharonim*), and in addition to them, there are those who do not want to accept the rulings of the earlier authorities (*Rishonim*), etc., based on the aforementioned argument.

The general answer to all this is that the Holy One, blessed be He, prepares the cure before the affliction. Nevertheless, the preparation is close to the time of the affliction, G-d forbid, as is proven by the subject matter and the evidence brought for this statement of our Sages.

Based on the above, the role of books chronicling the lives of the true great leaders of Israel also has a twofold purpose: not only "do good"—the communication of truth—but also "turn from evil," i.e., negating the desire to read books that falsify the history of the Jewish people.

This second goal requires that even matters which are seemingly secondary to the main subject should be included in biographies of the great leaders of Israel. People have become accustomed to these secondary details, and their absence will limit the circle of readers and interested parties, or diminish the authority of the book in the eyes of some readers. By these matters, I mean: photographs, facsimiles of manuscripts, and a table of important dates in the life of the individual, so that one is not forced to search for them throughout the chapters of the book, and so on. In several of your books, some of these details are missing, and in others, all are missing. If this suggestion of mine is accepted, I am sure you will include them in future books. It is also worth including them regarding those you have already published—in a second edition, or by printing a special booklet containing the **main** chapters of the lives of all those whose biographies you have printed. In any case, there is significant benefit in printing such a booklet.

With respect and blessing.

P.S.

My hope is strong that, as the well-known Mishnah says, he has a "good eye" regarding others and also regarding himself, and that he has fixed times every day for the study of the teachings of Chassidus. After all, it has already been said, "Do not hide yourself from your own flesh."

Incidentally, in your book *HaYehudi HaTov mi-Gostinin*, page 87, you bring his saying on the verse "Who can express the mighty acts of Hashem," etc. However, in one of the manuscripts of Chabad Chassidus—which appears to have been written by one of the Chassidim while our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe (author of the Tanya and Shulchan Aruch) was still alive—this is brought in the name of the Baal Shem Tov with a correction to the body of the saying: that it should be *yemallel* (express/speak), from the expression *mollin melilos* (rubbing ears of grain), which means breaking. Even though there are seventy faces to the Torah, it appears that in this specific case, this is how it must be (and not as copied in the book, in the sense of *milah*—cutting and tearing), because the interpretation of *mollin melilos* is based on the saying in the Zohar which is brought in many places in Chassidus (see Zohar I, 249a; Zohar II, 83a; *Biurei HaZohar* by the Mitteler Rebbe, *Vayechi* there; *Peirush HaTehillim* by the Tzemach Tzedek, 106b).

528

B"H, 6 Iyar, 5717

You surely know the statement of our Sages that even when "I am asleep in exile"—whether an external exile or even an internal one—even then, "my heart is awake" to Hashem, to His Torah, and to His mitzvos. One must find the appropriate words and speak them from the heart, "words that come from the heart," etc. And since this was said regarding all Jews, it is therefore a *rov* and a *chazakah*, etc.

With blessings for good tidings.

One should also learn from this that just as the speech of the Ribono shel Olam brings about an effect, a person must strive to ensure that his own words also bring about an effect. This depends on the person speaking, who is called *Adam* because of the concept of *adameh l'elyon* ("I will be like the Most High"—*Shelah*, Part *Torah Shebichtav*, *Parshas Vayeishev*, etc., page 172b). This is achieved when the words emerge from the heart, for then they enter the heart of the listener, as the well-known Chazal states, and through conduct according to the teaching of Chazal (*Berachos* 5b)—not only that they enter, but that they are also heeded.

## 530

530

B.H., 6 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Blessings and Peace!

In response to your letter from Rosh Chodesh Iyar, in which you write regarding the sudden change in the attitude of the person in question toward you, after you had become acquainted and met several times, and you wonder as to the reason for this.

As with all matters concerning a Jew, the true reason is a Torah-based and internal one; namely, that the conduct was not in accordance with the instructions of our holy Torah. Since this is a practical matter, one must state the facts as they are, so that one knows what to rectify: that this was, unfortunately, the opposite of the Torah’s instructions, and unfortunately, not always in accordance with *tzniyus*.

It is difficult to write such words, especially when one is obligated to judge everyone favorably. However, as stated above, since such an important matter depends on this, it is not useful to refrain from stating the matter as it is. This becomes even clearer from the outcome of the situation: a *richuk* (distancing) comes about through *kiruv ha'asur* (forbidden closeness).

As mentioned above, the intention of this letter is not to cause pain, *chas v'shalom*, or to make things difficult for anyone, but rather to awaken the *tikkun* (rectification) of the matter. This is the general concept of *teshuvah*, which helps in everything, provided it is done with truth and completeness. Regarding *HaKadosh Baruch Hu*, it is said, "Hashem looks at the heart," and when He—*Baruch Hu*—sees that there is a complete regret for the past and a *kabbalah tovah* (good resolution) for the future, as is necessary, then *Hashem Yisbarach* grants success in these matters.

After making a firm resolution regarding the above, one should find out from the side (i.e., not directly) whether there was perhaps also an external reason that caused the aforementioned change in attitude, and see what can be done about it. It may be that you will not even need to resort to that.

Since, according to your letter, you studied at Bais Yaakov, I assume that there are *brachos* and *tefillos* that you recite upon rising from sleep. It would be a proper thing if, on weekdays, you were to give a few cents to *tzedakah* before the *tefillos* that you recite.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, *shlita*,

Secretary

## 532

By the Grace of G-d
7 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greeting and Blessing,

In response to your letter—which did not indicate the time it was written—in which you ask regarding further proofs concerning the tradition of our ancestors, etc., and the like.

I am always surprised by such a question, which usually comes from those who have spent time studying secular sciences, particularly physics and the like. They claim that the proofs in these sciences are strong and precise, whereas regarding many matters of holiness, which depend only on faith, etc., they are not. My wonderment is that they do not realize the situation is **the exact opposite.** That is to say, the proofs for the matters and conclusions of the aforementioned sciences are incomparably weaker than the proofs that necessitate the validity of our holy Torah and its mitzvos.

Heaven forbid that I mean to say that the observance of Torah and mitzvos should be made dependent on the acceptance of proofs; that is not the way at all. As is well known, "we will do" preceded "we will hear," and it was precisely through this that they received the Torah (as in the discussion in Tractate Shabbos 88a, and its conclusion based on the verse, "The integrity of the upright shall guide them," etc.). I only intended to emphasize how baseless such claims are; they arise only from the *yetzer hara*. So great is the darkness of the *galus*, and primarily the stratagems of the *yetzer hara*, that it produces in many people a "spirit of folly"—in the well-known expression of our Sages—a folly that is the very opposite of sound reason.

Proofs from the perspective of the aforementioned sciences and disciplines regarding the necessity of observing Torah and mitzvos can be found in what is explained in the *Kuzari*, and also transcribed in the booklet *The Commandments* enclosed herewith.

It is understood that some may ask: since the proof is so absolute, why are there many people who do not observe Torah and mitzvos, whereas regarding the conclusions of secular sciences, whose proofs are weaker, the vast majority still accept them?

The answer is also simple: it is the nature of man, unfortunately, that if the reward and punishment come after a delay—and certainly if they come after a long time relative to a person’s life on earth—it is not enough to restrain him from fulfilling the desires and will of his soul and body. It is not enough to give him sufficient strength of spirit to behave differently from those around him, especially if they mock him. And when his *yetzer hatov* comes to explain to him that the end of such behavior is "bitter as wormwood"—as our Sages interpret this verse—he is not always successful in ensuring that the person will not be ashamed before the mockers, etc.

How wondrous it is that this very point—the strength of will not to be ashamed before mockers—is the beginning of all four parts of the *Shulchan Aruch*. From this, it is understood how deeply this matter touches upon the observance of all the laws of our Torah, the Torah of life. May it be His will that it be engraved in his mind that what is said, "Behold, it is a people that shall dwell alone," is the reality of every single one of the children of Israel. They have always been the fewest of all peoples, and they have always been distinguished, even in the most difficult *galus*, in all their ways. It is only through such conduct that they stand and exist until this very day, and soon they will merit the complete and true *geulah*.

Surely you know and observe the three daily study portions established for every soul by my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory—the *Chumash*, *Tehillim*, and *Tanya*—and in any case, you should observe them from now on.

With blessing,

On behalf of the Rebbe,
Secretary

P.S. Regarding what you wrote asking about the conduct concerning bread taken from one who desecrates the Shabbos: there are several details involved, and you should ask a rabbi who is a *moreh hora'ah* in your area after detailing the specifics to him.

## 533

B.H., 7 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, learned, and distinguished individual, engaged in communal affairs,
Rabbi Alter Meir, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings!

...Regarding what you wrote at the end of your letter, that because you did not find the source for the purity of the food in Mitzrayim, you cited the conventional version.

I am surprised by this, for it is understood that precisely when one finds a formula that is not common at all, it surely has a reliable source, since the concern is high that its innovation would arouse astonishment and questioning.

The reason I am dwelling on this detail is, as understood from the introduction, that generally, those who look for sources for unusual formulas are satisfied—most of them, if not all—with merely pointing to a specific place where the uncommon version is found. In my opinion, this is the secondary part in comparison to the primary issue that requires explanation. And that is: the inclusion of an uncommon formula certainly has a specific reason, in that the matter being discussed is strengthened and supported specifically by that uncommon and known formula, which is not the case with the conventional version.

In the vast majority of cases, this is more difficult to explain than it is to find the source. Furthermore, this clarifies the matter more, by revealing how precise the author of the article or the writer of the book was; this is not the case regarding the source—we are not dealing with fools, and when they bring a statement of our Sages, they certainly saw it in a book, etc.

From the above, it is also understood that those who wish to "correct" a conventional formula in place of an uncommon one—even though in rare cases it is possible that the uncommon formula is a copyist's error—in the vast majority of cases this is not so. Since the author of the statement was precise with the uncommon formula, the "correction" is not a repair, but the opposite.

And so it is in the present case, where I intended precisely to bring the formula in which food is mentioned, in connection with the struggle in recent times specifically regarding this matter, which has taken on distressing and even shameful forms, and most especially in Eretz Yisrael, may it be rebuilt and established. It is not appropriate to elaborate on a painful matter, and a word to the wise is sufficient.

I have elaborated on all of the above, even though one does not cry over the past, for the reason that if you find a way to correct the aforementioned in my opinion, it is worthwhile, and perhaps this too is for the good, since through the correction the intention may be highlighted more.

Regarding the substance of the matter, the source in the Midrash for the uncommon formula—which is also the source for the conventional version that they did not change their dress and their language—is noted in the margin.

May it be the will of Hashem that very soon we merit the fulfillment of the prophecy, "Behold, it is a people that shall dwell alone," for the preparation and vessel for this [and according to the statement of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe in Tanya, beginning of Chapter 37, that the ultimate perfection of the days of Moshiach, etc., depends on our deeds and our avodah throughout the duration of the galus, it is understood that a specific detail in perfection depends on a detail of this nature in our avodah; and likewise, the matter of "dwelling alone" for the future depends on the "dwelling alone" during the time of the galus, by the fact that Bnei Yisrael are distinct in all their affairs]. And may we merit, through this, to continue in the Sefiras HaOmer the matter of longing for this, for the fulfillment of this prophecy, for the longing adds courage, vitality, and light in the fulfillment of all the matters that hasten and draw closer the thing for which we long: our true and complete redemption.

With respect and blessings for good tidings.

In the notes of R. S. Buber (Pesikta d'Rav Kahana, Chapter 10), the conventional statement that they did not change their **dress** (the aforementioned conventional version) has no trace anywhere. See also *Otzar Divrei Chachamim* by Rabbi Heiman, s.v. *Bizchus*. However, the publisher noted that in that Pesikta, in the margin, it is found in *Lekach Tov*, Parshas Tavo (26:5): it is noted there that it was their **dress, their food,** and their language that were different from the Mitzriyim. Note the *Yalkut Shimoni* on the aforementioned verse, "Behold, it is a people that shall dwell alone." See also *Miluim* to *Torah Sheleimah*, Parshas Shemos, p. 229.

***

**533**

**That you did not find the source:** Referring to the letter 537 above: that the Yidden were distinct there in their dress, their food, and their language.

**Cited the conventional version:** In the reproduction of the content of the aforementioned letter in the newspapers.

## 534

B"H, 8 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

Mrs. Esther, may she live,

Blessings and peace!

In response to your letter, in which you write the main points of your life and that you are now working as a secretary in an institution...

Even though you do not specify the nature of the secretarial duties at this institution, there is no doubt that this role also provides the opportunity to spread and instill, more and more, the spirit of traditional Judaism in the students of the institution. This is especially true since the director of the institution is interested in this and does what he can in this direction. This is analogous to all matters of our world, which, although they are many, nevertheless combine into groups, and groups into kinds, and kinds into categories, until ultimately, everyone who believes in unity and in one G-d unifies all the phenomena of the world into one matter and one point: the unity of the Creator in every detail of creation.

In this same way, it is also with a person’s life and their actions in life. Although they are many, they combine into kinds, and kinds into categories, leading to the final and innermost point of all actions and the entire duration of a person’s life, in the phrasing of our Sages of blessed memory: "I was created to serve my Maker"—meaning, the fulfillment of the Creator’s will in every matter and detail, as the verse instructs: "In all your ways, know Him." There is an additional responsibility for all those who can influence the education of the younger generation, since this influence can direct the student and the one being influenced in the true, desired direction for their benefit, or, G-d forbid, the opposite. According to the conduct of the Creator, blessed be He, who is good and upright, He provides in advance the strengths and possibilities to fulfill even this most responsible role, and the matter depends only on the will of the person.

It is obvious that I did not come to deliver a sermon in the aforementioned lines, but rather in connection with what you conclude in your letter regarding guidance and conduct, and that Hashem should add to you happiness, etc.

I wish to point out and awaken you: fortunate is your portion that you have been situated in a way that you can act to strengthen and spread Judaism. This is the path, the channel, and the vessel for receiving the blessings of Hashem, including what is needed for your own personal matters, those of your husband, and those of your entire family, may they live.

I am sure you know and observe the custom of the daughters of Israel from time immemorial: to set aside money for tzedakah before lighting the candles every Erev Shabbos and Erev Yom Tov.

With blessings for good tidings in all that was mentioned.

P.S.
It is understood that since your husband, may he live, also works in the field of education, the aforementioned words are directed to him as well, and may it be Hashem's will that I succeed in having them achieve their intended effect.

## 536

B"H, 8 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter which has just been received, I am writing back immediately, as you mention that you intend to leave your country soon.

This reasoning does not seem correct to me at all. Since Hashem has blessed you with special talents in the field of kosher chinuch, and you are located in a country where the shortage of personnel in this area is alarming—as is evident from the tangible, terrible consequences of this shortage on the state of Yiddishkeit in that country—therefore, according to the well-known command of our Sages, "the poor of your city take precedence." In my opinion, you have both the responsibility and the privilege to search with the necessary diligence for opportunities to work in the field of chinuch within the country where you currently reside.

Since you do not state the reason for this current change and your new plan, which is the opposite of what you had in mind some time ago, I do not grasp the details of the situation in... but in truth, this does not matter at all regarding the practical outcome. The two points mentioned are absolutely true: your talents, as well as the talents of your wife, tichyeh, in the field of kosher chinuch, and the terrible situation that cries out for dedicated and capable educators in that country.

It is possible that the terms of the salary also contributed to your new line of reasoning, although I consider that a remote concern. Nevertheless, in my opinion, Hashem, whose attribute is measure for measure, will surely bless all those who engage in bringing the children of Israel closer in that country through their kosher education, providing them with everything they need, from soul to body.

With blessings for good tidings regarding all the above.

## 537

537

B"H, 8 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the Kfar Chabad Committee,
May Hashem preserve them and grant them life,

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter dated 24 Nissan, along with the enclosure.

It is surprising that you are brief regarding the *kinus* (gathering) and the *kabbalas panim* (reception), even though this is a matter regarding which it has been said that one should elaborate. May it be Hashem’s will that this brevity in writing does not lead to a corresponding brevity in the actual results regarding the *kinus* and the *kabbalas panim*.

Regarding your question as to what I intended concerning a *gemach* (free-loan fund) in Kfar Chabad:

I am puzzled by the question, as the matter is straightforward. Since all the residents of the village complain about a lack of funds to finance various matters, even if it is only in the form of a loan, why are you satisfied with a *gemach* fund on a small scale? At the same time, there are ways to organize a *gemach* fund as needed for the village’s requirements. As I also wrote in my previous letter, based on my conversation with R' Z.R. *sheyichyeh*, who told me clearly that there are specific banks that provide loans for the working capital of small business owners and the like—he also told me that he suggested to one of the *gemach* funds in the village that if they would maintain accounting books and the like, he would see to it that one of the offices over which he has influence would contribute a significant sum in order to expand the *gemach* enterprise. Certainly, there are other possibilities of this nature. In general, it is surprising that such matters need to be learned about in the United States, at a time when several new villages have been established in Eretz Yisrael *tichyeh* recently, including villages of people who are extremely impoverished. Surely it is impossible that one cannot learn from all these villages regarding methods and approaches concerning a *gemach* in Kfar Chabad and regarding the management of economic affairs in general.

When I spoke with the esteemed Chossid R' A. Drizin *sheyichyeh*, I did not yet know the above, and therefore I suggested, based on the information I had at the time, that there are offices that participate in *gemach* funds up to a "lira for a lira" basis. If each of the village residents would contribute a certain amount as a *gemach* for a few years to establish a *gemach* fund, and they would exert the necessary energy with the aforementioned offices, this would constitute a fund that would have a significant sum at its disposal for *gemach* purposes.

It is understood that all of the above does not in any way affect the continued existence of the current *gemach* funds, especially as you describe in this letter the activities of the current *gemach* funds, which are not in the area mentioned above. On the contrary, one must endeavor not to abolish the existing *gemach* funds, for several reasons.

It is also understood that all of the above does not diminish the importance of the matter you mention at the end of your letter, that you intend to approach the Jewish Agency, etc., regarding loans, etc.

Regarding what you wrote about the matter of the orchards, the chicken coop, etc.:

One should investigate this with experts in this field who are on-site. Certainly, as mentioned above, there are several villages whose situation in previous years was similar, and investigating how they acted to rectify the situation can be of great help regarding Kfar Chabad.

With blessings for success in establishing Kfar Chabad and developing it in all directions, both materially and spiritually. I look forward to hearing good news.

... You must explain to the mother, may she live, of the aforementioned person, that one should not conduct oneself with the Holy One, blessed be He, in such a manner—setting conditions, that if a certain matter is fulfilled, then one will give tzedakah. A Yid conducts himself in the opposite order: first one gives tzedakah, and afterwards one asks of Hashem, not as a condition, but as a request—that He, blessed be He, who is the essence of goodness, should fulfill the heartfelt desire. And by having strong bitachon in this, one then sees it with physical eyes as well, and very soon. Hopefully, you will find the appropriate words to explain to her what has been said above.

With blessings for good tidings,

## 5441

B"H, 11 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

To Mr. Yitzchak Haskin,

Shalom u’Bracha!

For some time now, I have intended to write to you, but due to the magnitude of my preoccupations, it did not come to fruition.

I wish to express my satisfaction with your visit when you were here, and for the opportunity to meet you face-to-face. I was particularly pleased to hear from you that a noticeable change for the better is evident in your attitude toward matters of religion, and that you are utilizing your influence in this regard to the best of your ability. Most importantly, regarding the future, you have plans and prospects to [...] and to increase your efforts in this. May the Ribono shel Olam help you to fulfill the desires of your heart in this as soon as possible. The merit of the many who benefit from assistance in matters of religion—our Torah, the Torah of life—will assist you in succeeding in this, as well as in your personal affairs. This is especially true given that the merit of your ancestors also assists you, as you told me of your origins and lineage, hailing from Rabbonim and Chassidim.

Based on the point we discussed—that even a physical thing is never entirely lost, and certainly this is true of a spiritual thing, and the only difference is whether it is utilized with the greatest possible efficiency—I am confident that your father bequeathed to you the trait of dedication to everything holy in Yisrael, which is the tradition of our ancestors from the standing at Har Sinai. May it be His will that you go from strength to strength to reveal this dedication to the holy matters of our people in an ever-increasing measure.

Since, according to the teachings of the Baal Shem Tov, every matter occurs through Hashgacha Pratis, it is surely no coincidence that our first meeting—and I hope there will be a good continuation—took place on the eve of the seventh day of Pesach, the day of the splitting of the Yam Suf. The unique quality of this day is to strengthen in every individual, and within our people as a whole, the recognition that Yidden are not intimidated by physical or natural obstacles and hindrances; for when they go to receive the Torah, just as it was then, so too now, the seas split before them.

With respect and blessing.

## 542

By the Grace of G-d
11 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings,

After a long hiatus, I received your letter of 27 Nissan, in which you write that you returned once again to the yeshiva... and after one month, you changed your mind about this and took a position as a teacher in... and now you have decided that you do not want this either.

In general, such conduct indicates a total lack of *kabalas ol* (accepting the yoke), to the point of lacking order in essential matters—and certainly, *kal v’chomer*, in secondary matters. It is understood that such conduct is untenable. It is also understood that the remedy is not in the heavens, nor is it difficult, for it depends only on contemplating for a brief moment the fact that each of us was created for a specific purpose—as the Mishnah states, to serve one’s Creator. Consequently, laziness and following one’s own desires indicate that one does not think he must accomplish anything for the aforementioned purpose of serving Hashem.

Furthermore, it is simple and understood that one cannot settle into life as one ought to if one decides to follow one’s own desires—and the desire here is not to exert oneself, not to *anstrengen* (exert effort) in any matter, whether in learning or in a job, etc. For apparently, this is the common denominator in the reasons you left the yeshiva previously and now again, and that you do not want the teaching position, because apparently, it would have required changing your habit of lacking order in life, and you do not want to.

It is understood that my intention here is not merely to preach morality, but to awaken you once and for all. A person cannot declare himself an exception to the rule, to the vast majority of those who settle into life, regarding whom it is said: "If you have not exerted yourself and found, do not believe it." It is imperative that you contemplate once again why you do not want the teaching position, and contemplate what will be when you begin in agriculture—as it is possible that you will be dissatisfied with this as well (a hypothesis close to reality). This should be done by inquiring among those engaged in agriculture about the nature of the work, and after this contemplation, to work with energy, to the point of exertion appropriate to the words of our Sages, in the profession you choose. Do not deceive yourself that by acting contrary to the way of the world for a few days, you will see open miracles in matters of *parnassah*. Rather, hope for the fulfillment of the promise of our Sages: "You have exerted yourself and found." May it be the will of the Almighty that He place in your heart the resolve to decide what is good before Him, in both material and spiritual matters alike.

Everything mentioned above also applies to the conclusion of your letter, where you write that your spiritual state is "terrible," that you have abandoned your studies, etc., and that you want to do *teshuvah*, etc. It requires great deliberation whether such a state should be called "terrible," since the *yetzer hara* has only a small part in your situation; it stems only from laziness and a lack of desire to exert yourself (*anstrengen*) in any matter, etc. [Though in truth, this too stems from the *yetzer hara*, because in the realm of holiness, the matter of *zerizus* (alacrity) is among the essential matters; however, this is not the *yetzer hara* as it is commonly understood]. It is easier to overcome this, since the conduct of laziness and negligence is also the opposite of simple human reason, not only the opposite of the reason of a person called "intellectual" or the reason of the *nefesh habehamis* specifically.

Regarding what you wrote about the *shidduch* with someone who has the same name as your mother: you should only consider this *shidduch* if one of them adds a name, and furthermore, that they do not live in the same place.

You do not mention anything about your participation in the activities of Tzeirei Agudas Chabad during the past month of Nissan; perhaps this is only a deficiency in writing, and not in action.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe,
Secretary

***

**Add a name:** See also above, Vol. 8, p. 485, and the references in the notes there. Vol. 11, p. 3414. Vol. 14, p. 226. Below, p. 520.

## 5,345

B"H, 13 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter from Thursday, along with the enclosed raffle list. It is surprising that you did not acknowledge receipt of my letter inquiring why you did not visit Yerushalayim Ir HaKodesh (may it be rebuilt and established) to deliver a *pan* from here. Even though others have done so, it is known from our Sages that one does not learn from everyone. This is especially true regarding the delivery of a *pan*, where each person delivers "what they have taken more of," and surely their perspectives are not the same. Furthermore, the main point is that even if we were to assume that what others deliver would lack nothing for those who hear it, it is certainly missing the *inyan* for the one who is required to deliver the *pan*. And who is greater than our Rebbeim, our Nesi'im? It is known that the *nusach* of the *pan* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe (the Rebbe Rashab) of blessed memory, which he wrote before saying *da"ch*, was "that they should effect [a change] in me and in those who hear."

Surely those who are in Eretz HaKodesh (may it be rebuilt and established) do not require special awakening to utilize the bright day of Lag B'Omer that is approaching. Nevertheless, I mention this as well, if only to fulfill the adage: "It is only needed for the sake of adding more."

With blessings for good tidings in all the above, in good health.

**M. Schneerson**
