I received regards from you through the distinguished and G-d-fearing student of Tomchei Temimim, Chanoch Hendel Lieberman, and also through the distinguished and G-d-fearing individual, involved in communal needs and multi-faceted in his activities, R' Avraham Dov Kramer, who also told me about your state of health.

At an auspicious time, I will mention you at the holy gravesite of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, for an improvement in your health. This is especially relevant since you had the merit of studying in the Yeshiva Tomchei Temimim; nothing is ever lost, certainly not in physical matters, and all the more so in spiritual matters. This is especially true given that our holy Rebbes, of blessed memory, invested so much effort into that Yeshiva.

It is therefore certain that even now they are arousing great compassion for each and every one of the students in whatever they may need, particularly regarding health in both physical and spiritual terms. As is known from the statement of our Sages, of blessed memory, the 248 limbs of a person correspond to the 248 positive mitzvos, and the 365 sinews correspond to the 365 negative mitzvos. Being a doctor yourself, it is surely unnecessary to emphasize that if even one limb is not as it should be, one must not tire in healing it. How much more so is this true in spiritual matters, with the addition—as the Alter Rebbe writes—that there are physical ailments for which no medicine helps, but in spiritual matters, there is no such thing that the healing of the soul cannot cure, to the point that it can even be rectified in such a way as if the ailment (weakness) had never existed. "There is nothing that can stand before the will," and "There is nothing that can stand before teshuvah."

I am certain that it is unnecessary to elaborate on the above, only to add one point which is surely also known to you: the emphasis of the teachings of Chassidus from the Baal Shem Tov and our holy Rebbes of Chabad, that in all of a person’s affairs, there must be "Serve Hashem with joy." When one is joyful that Hashem gives one the opportunity to serve Him, one sees success in all matters, both physical and spiritual.

Awaiting good news regarding all the above, and with wishes for the approaching holiday of Shavuos, together with all of Klal Yisrael, to receive the Torah according to the expression and blessing of my father-in-law, the Rebbe: to receive the Torah with joy and with inwardness.

With blessings for a complete recovery.

## 582

B”H, 25 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and distinguished R’ Yisrael Moshe, may he live:

Shalom u’Bracha!

In response to your letter, in which you ask whether a *heter iska* is required when you are given a loan from the *mosdos* in Eretz Yisrael, may it be rebuilt and established soon.

This depends on the manner in which the loan is structured. In general, it is always better to execute a *heter iska* in order to avoid any doubt, especially since one does not always know the specific details of the loan.

Regarding skipping parts of *davening*, even from the beginning:

The instruction is well-known: regardless of any *heterim* that may exist regarding this, in practice, one does not do so. After all, one can arrive at *shul* earlier in order to keep pace with the *minyan*.

It appears that you are thinking about traveling to our Holy Land, may it be built and established speedily in our days through Moshiach Tzidkeinu. Surely, I need not point out to you what is written in the *Tashbetz Katan*, that one must take upon oneself a special *zehirus* (diligence) in matters of Torah and *mitzvos*. For it is not the same when one finds oneself in the King’s palace as when one is outside of it. Although the obligation to fulfill Torah and *mitzvos* exists in every place and at every time, it is not the same in the King’s palace, etc. This is especially true since the journey to Eretz Yisrael is undertaken by the free choice of a Yid who seeks to obtain the necessary documents and permits from human beings. All the more so, one must prepare to arrive in such a place as Eretz Yisrael—a land upon which "the eyes of Hashem your G-d are always upon it, from the beginning of the year to the end of the year"—with the necessary *zehirus* in matters of Torah and *mitzvos*.

May it be His will that you report good news regarding all of the above.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 580

B.H., 27 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter from Thursday, which concludes with twelve questions and a note that there is more to ask, but there is no end to such things.

Beyond the fact that it is impossible to answer twelve such questions in a letter, the main point is that once these twelve are answered, the *yetzer* will surely present others in their place, and in even greater numbers.

The main thing is that for a *bochur* of your age, the task is not to seek out questions and think that the reason you lack *hasmadah* and *shekidah* is because you have not received answers to all your questions. The essence of a *ben yeshivah* in general is to learn Torah and to desire to understand what is written in it—and certainly not to "dig" (*griblen*) to see if one can invent a *kushya* that others have not noticed, and to investigate who might be able to resolve it.

Consider the analogy of someone who begins to study the science of medicine, *lehavdil*: the day after starting, he informs his teachers that they must explain to him why, when treating this or that condition, they prescribe bitter pills—perhaps the pills could be sweet? Why is surgery necessary—let them heal without surgery, etc., and so on. And regardless of the fact that you write that you have already been studying in *yeshivah* for two years, in relation to the depth contained in our holy Torah, this is perhaps further removed than the knowledge of a first-day medical student is to the knowledge of a fully qualified doctor.

It is understood that I do not mean to say that one should not understand what is written in the Torah; on the contrary, it is an obligation to learn Torah specifically with the intent to understand it. However, there must be the precedence of *naaseh* before *nishma*—a matter of *kabbalas ol*. When that exists, one does not allow oneself to be distracted by the *yetzer* from learning Torah with *hasmadah* and *shekidah*. Then, even if there is some *kushya*, it does not affect one’s *hasmadah* and *shekidah*. Especially when you review those twelve *kushyos* again, as you yourself write at the beginning of your letter, these are matters that the great Torah scholars of Israel have certainly addressed. We know that they learned Torah with *hasmadah* and *shekidah* and fulfilled *mitzvos* with the greatest *hiddur*. From this, it is understood: 1) that there is a satisfying answer, and 2) if there is a detail that is not fully understood by the intellect, it does not affect at all the conduct of how a Jew must behave according to the Torah. For what is the wonder that a human does not understand what Hashem understands, *kavyachol*? On the contrary, the wonder is the opposite: that there are matters from the wisdom and will of Hashem—which is the Torah—that a human, who is a limited being, understands with his limited intellect.

May it be Hashem’s will that you abandon the path of "digging" (*griblen zich*) and searching for *kushyos*, which is entirely not your concern, and that you dedicate yourself to fulfilling the statement of the *Mishnah*: "I was created to serve my Maker." Especially as we approach the time of the giving of our Torah, which, as mentioned above, was accepted through the precedence of *naaseh* before *nishma*. As our Sages of blessed memory said, this is precisely how it must be, for then "the integrity of the upright shall guide them," and if otherwise, *chas v'shalom*, etc. (see *Maseches Shabbos* 88a).

With blessings for good tidings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 5787

B"H, 28 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing, and diligent worker in communal affairs, R' Yisrael, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter of 25 Iyar and the enclosure.

You will surely find the words to explain to [the person mentioned] that his claim—that he cannot study now because he is in pain—is akin to a sick person saying he cannot take medicine because he is ill. Our Sages have already informed us that the Torah brings healing to the world, and that one who feels pain in his head or body should occupy himself with Torah. Even though it is understood that deep, analytical study is difficult when the body is in pain, one must nevertheless make an effort in this regard, and certainly, at the very least, in study for the sake of reading through the text. At the minimum, he should keep to the three daily study portions established for every person by my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory—the well-known portions of Chumash, Tehillim, and Tanya. May it be G-d’s will that you bring good news that your words came from the heart and entered his heart, and that they led to actual results.

And very soon, may you also bring good news regarding his personal matter, as he has already taken the natural step of visiting a doctor. Regarding what the aforementioned person says—that it will take a long time—this is certainly not the case. As is well known, the precision of our Sages’ teaching is that the Torah gave the doctor permission to heal, but not, G-d forbid, to delay the healing. I await good news regarding all of the above.

With blessings in the style of the Rav, my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, for the reception of the Torah with joy and inwardness, and for the success of both of you regarding the day camp as well.

## 5788

B"H, 28 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing and upright, scion of a noble lineage,
R' Avraham Yehuda, may he live and be well,

Peace and blessing!

In response to your letter of 23 Iyar, regarding the handwritten booklets (*bichlech*) and their arrangement, and the clarification of which *maamarim* therein have been printed and which have not, etc.

I do not know who among *anash* in the Holy Land—may it be rebuilt and established—is expert in such matters. It is a pity that they were brought to the Holy Land initially rather than being sent here. Although I am overwhelmed with burdens—and unfortunately, not only burdens of joy—had they been in close proximity here, someone else could have been occupied with this. I would have been able, at the very least, to be in a position to oversee it from time to time, and to inquire on the spot whether there is an expert in these matters and to discuss it with him.

Certainly, you are also aware of the conduct of Chassidim from generation to generation, who did not concern themselves "so much" with who the author of a *maamar* was, or whether it had been printed or not. As is known, the expression of some of *anash* is that all the *maamarim* of our *Nesi'im* are like *Halacha L'Moshe MiSinai*, as per the well-known statement of our Sages. What difference does it make if this *halacha* was revealed by this *Nasi* or that *Nasi*? Especially since it is understood that this does not have the same practical implications as in the *nigleh* (the revealed Torah), where there are many rules such as "the *halacha* follows so-and-so over so-and-so" or "the *halacha* follows the later authorities." None of this applies to the *nistar* (the hidden Torah), where there are no difficulties or disputes, etc.

Although I personally reject this approach entirely—and on several occasions I spoke about this with my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory—and it is understood that he did not subscribe to this aforementioned approach either, nevertheless, it seems this is one of those things where "Chassidim conduct themselves as they conduct themselves." May it be the will of Hashem that they never have any greater shortcomings or errors than this...

With the blessing in the language of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, to receive the Torah with joy and with inwardness.

***

**R' Avraham Yehuda, may he live and be well:** Of Jerusalem. Additional letters to him appear above in Vol. 13, 4703, and in the references noted in the footnotes there.

**Handwritten booklets (*bichlech*):** From the estate of his father, R' Dovid Tzvi Hirsch (R' D"tz). See above, Vol. 9, letter 2669, and the references noted in the footnotes there.

## 589

589

B"H, 29 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

It was with great astonishment that I learned of your custom to fast from time to time on Mondays and Thursdays, as well as on Erev Rosh Chodesh. This is despite the well-known instruction of the Baal Shem Tov that one must ensure the body remains healthy so that it does not hinder one’s ability to fulfill the directive to serve Hashem Yisbarach with joy. Indeed, we see in our generation that frequent fasting weakens one’s health, which subsequently becomes an obstacle to the performance of mitzvos.

It goes without saying that this refers to fasts that one takes upon oneself (as opposed to the fasts prescribed by the Shulchan Aruch). If you specifically desire a practice of this nature, the instruction of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, is well known: this can be achieved by refraining from speaking when it is unnecessary, even when one has a strong desire to do so—and this applies not only to lashon hara, but even to idle chatter. Furthermore, "fasting" from food can also be practiced by eating foods that are beneficial to one’s health, while not specifically seeking out foods simply for the sake of their taste.

I hope that these few lines will be sufficient for you to rectify the aforementioned practice. Since I understand that you have conducted yourself in this manner more than three times, you must perform hataras nedarim (the annulment of vows), and thereafter replace the fasts from food with the aforementioned "fasts" of speech and diet. May Hashem Yisbarach assist you in all of this, and together with your husband, may you lead your son in the Chassidic path, in good health and with joy.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 590

B"H, 29 Iyar, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the avreich Moshe Hillel, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

In response to your letters from the 6th and 22nd of Iyar, it gave me pleasure to read that you are guiding your class not only in their studies, but also in matters of yiras shamayim and Chassidus. In truth, the two are interdependent, as our Alter Rebbe writes that ahavah is the root of all 248 positive mitzvos, and yirah is the root of all 365 negative mitzvos (see carefully Tanya, beginning of Chapter 4). This is especially true when one is in our Holy Land—may it be built and established by Moshiach Tzidkeinu speedily in our days—upon which the eyes of Hashem your G-d are from the beginning of the year to the end of the year. From this, it is also understood that any effort and toil in this regard is worthwhile, for this is the whole of man, as it is written: "Fear G-d and keep His commandments, for this is the whole of man." And even one who sustains only one nefesh of Yisrael, it is as if he has sustained an entire world.

It is also understood that in order to influence others, it is not enough to be merely "moist" [with knowledge/inspiration]; one must be "moist in order to moisten" others. The analogy is understood.

With blessing,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 5717

By the Grace of Hashem, 1st of Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Rabbi and Chassid, engaged in communal affairs, etc.,
R' Efraim Eliezer HaKohen, may he live and be well,

Greetings and blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter from the 3rd of Parshas Naso, along with the enclosure.

Yesterday, when I was at the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, I mentioned you. Since "no holiness is ever uprooted from its place" (see *Iggeres HaKodesh*, Epistle 27, for the explanation), it is understood that a Nasi of Israel, even after his soul has ascended on high, continues to stand and arouse Divine mercy for all those who are connected to him and for their affairs.

Since today is the day they arrived at the wilderness of Sinai, I will conclude with a matter relevant to the day, in the language of my father-in-law, the Rebbe: May we receive the Torah with joy and with inwardness (*pnimiyus*).

With blessings,

**M. Schneerson**

P.S.
Enclosed are the Torah insights of your son-in-law, may he live. For the sake of affection, I have added a few hurried comments, as time does not permit more.

In *Bechoros* 12b, we learned in the Mishnah: "The *arachim* (vows of valuation) are at their proper time; *pidyon haben* (redemption of the firstborn) is after thirty days; the redemption of a *peter chamor* (firstborn donkey) is immediately." The Gemara raises a difficulty and cites a *baraita*: "There is no *arachim*, *pidyon haben*, *nezirus*, or *peter chamor* less than thirty days, and one may add [to the time] forever." Rav Nachman says this means that if one redeemed it [before thirty days], it is redeemed; *l'chatchila* one should wait until thirty days, like *pidyon haben*, but *b'dieved* it is redeemed immediately. However, the primary mitzvah of *pidyon* is not fulfilled, as Rashi explains there. Rav Sheshes answered that it means one does not transgress [the prohibition of delaying the mitzvah] until thirty days; meaning, the mitzvah of *pidyon* is essentially immediate, and one does not transgress until thirty days have passed, and from then on, one transgresses. Rami bar Chama challenged Rav Sheshes from the *baraita* which states: "Its mitzvah is all thirty days; from then on, if he redeems it, he redeems it; if not, he decapitates it." Does this not mean that the mitzvah is to delay it for all thirty days, as Rav Nachman says? This poses a difficulty for Rav Sheshes. The Gemara answers: "No, its mitzvah is not to redeem it for all thirty days." The Gemara then asks: If so, from then on, should it not have said "either he redeems it or he transgresses"? The Gemara concludes: "Rather, Rava said: It is not difficult; this [the first *baraita*] is according to Rabbi Elazar, and this [the second] is according to the Sages." Rashi explains: "Rather, Rava said: The mitzvah is to delay it, and even so, we do not answer according to Rav Nachman, and it is not difficult. The fact that it states 'all thirty days' is a mitzvah to delay it, and this is according to Rabbi Elazar, who equates the firstborn of an animal to the firstborn of a human. And the fact that it states 'immediately' is according to the Sages, who do not equate them."

In the Rambam, *Hilchos Bikkurim* 12:6, he writes: "From when is one obligated to redeem it? From birth until thirty days. After thirty days, if he wishes, he decapitates it; if he wishes to redeem it, he redeems it, and there is nothing here but a mitzvah of delay alone." The *Kesef Mishneh* writes that it is known that the *halacha* follows Rav Sheshes in matters of prohibitions, and therefore he concludes, "and there is nothing here but a mitzvah of delay alone," which is the "transgression" that Rav Sheshes speaks of. This is difficult: if he rules like Rav Sheshes, how could he write the *baraita* from which they challenged Rav Sheshes in its original wording, "either he redeems it or he decapitates it," whereas for Rav Sheshes they challenged him, "should it not have said 'or he transgresses'?" and they did not answer that point. This is the difficulty raised by the Maharit Algazi (Mahari Korkos).

Now, in *Halacha* 1, the Rambam writes: "And if he did not wish to redeem it, it is a positive commandment to decapitate it, as it is said, 'But if you do not redeem it, then you shall decapitate it.'" The Ra'avad objects there, saying that decapitation is not a mitzvah, but rather a transgression, and he is called a damager and he causes the loss of the Kohen's property. The *Ritva Algazi* writes that these two opinions stem from the *Mechilta* on Parshas Bo: "If you do not redeem it, then you shall decapitate it—from here they said: the mitzvah of *pidyon* takes precedence over the mitzvah of decapitation. Another interpretation: If you do not redeem it, decapitate it; since you caused the loss of the Kohen's property, your property shall also be lost." The first interpretation is the Mishnah on 13a, that the mitzvah of *pidyon* takes precedence over the mitzvah of decapitation. The second is what Levi taught on 10b: "He caused the loss of the Kohen's property, therefore his property shall be lost." This is the view of the Ra'avad, while the Rambam rules according to the Mishnah on 13a.

Rashi explains Levi's teaching: "Therefore his property shall be lost—the Beis Din will force him to decapitate it after thirty days, as is explained in the chapter, and it will be forbidden for benefit." The *Acharonim* ask: From where do we know that Beis Din forces him? And if it is based on the law of "we beat him until his soul departs" for all positive commandments, why would Beis Din not force him to perform the *pidyon*, since *pidyon* takes precedence over decapitation? It seems that Rashi was troubled by the contradiction between Levi's teaching and the Mishnah on 13a. Levi's teaching implies that decapitation is a penalty, while the Mishnah implies it is a mitzvah. Therefore, Rashi holds that both are true. How? Decapitation is a mitzvah upon Beis Din to adjudicate the penalty of decapitation, just like all laws of penalties, which it is a mitzvah for Beis Din to adjudicate. If it is a mitzvah upon Beis Din, then there must be a law that after a set time they must adjudicate the penalty; otherwise, when would they adjudicate the penalty, if he can redeem it at any moment? This is also precise in the language of the Gemara: "He caused the loss of the Kohen's property." It is difficult: why call it "caused the loss" if he can still redeem it? Rather, it must be that after a certain time, the penalty of decapitation begins, and that is the meaning of the phrase "he caused the loss of the Kohen's property." Therefore, Rashi explains that Beis Din forces him to decapitate it after thirty days, as we have explained, that it is a mitzvah upon Beis Din to adjudicate the penalty of decapitation.

After these words, let us examine the language of the Rambam, who wrote in *Halacha* 6: "And after thirty days, if he wishes to decapitate it, he decapitates it, and if he wishes to redeem it, he redeems it, and there is nothing here but a mitzvah of delay alone." This is difficult: he ruled in *Halacha* 1 that the mitzvah of *pidyon* takes precedence over the mitzvah of decapitation, so why did he write decapitation before *pidyon* here? Rather, these are the very things we have written: the mitzvah of decapitation begins after a set time (and the Sages estimated this as after thirty days). Therefore, the Rambam wrote that after thirty days, decapitation takes precedence over *pidyon*, and his source is the teaching of Levi, as we explained. In this, he differs from Rashi and holds that decapitation is a mitzvah just like *pidyon*.

According to this, the difficulty of the Mahari Korkos is resolved in a good way: the language of the Rambam is not the language of the *baraita* from which Rav Sheshes was challenged. He was precise in his language and wrote that decapitation takes precedence over *pidyon* to say that *pidyon* has a time limit, and after that time, the mitzvah of decapitation begins. This is exactly the words of Rav Sheshes, who said that one transgresses after thirty days.

Regarding what I heard as a statement from my father-in-law, may he live, in the name of the Lubavitcher Rebbe, may he live, concerning the matter of giving gifts to a bride and groom on their wedding day: I thought of a simple path—since the Sages said that all his sins are forgiven, he is in the state of "before the sin" and "before the decree of 'by the sweat of your brow you shall eat bread,'" and consequently, his livelihood is without toil.

## 5192

B"H, 1 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings,

In response to your letter, in which you ask based on what is brought in Chapter 1 of the holy Tanya, that the souls of the nations of the world are from the *kelipos* that are entirely impure—yet they were commanded with the seven *mitzvos*, etc.

It is explained in *Chassidus* that the souls of the *chasidei umos ha'olam* (the pious among the nations) are from the good within *kelipas nogah*. See *Likutei Be'urim* by the great Rabbi, the great Chassid and great *tzaddik*, etc., R. Hillel of Paritch, page 47d; and in Rambam, *Hilchos Melachim*, end of Chapter 8, that one who accepts the seven *mitzvos* is considered among the *chasidei umos ha'olam*.

Regarding the matter of converts—see *Shabbos* 146a, that their *mazal* was present at Har Sinai. See also *Midbar Kados* (by the Chida), entry *Gerim*.

Regarding what you write about matters of *shidduchim*, that you have an older brother: you should ask your brother to forgive you completely, and then you too may involve yourself in pursuing a respectable proposal.

I trust that you keep the three *shiurim* that are equal for every soul, established by my father-in-law, the Rebbe, *ztzukulleh"h, nbg"m, zy"a*—the *Chumash*, *Tehillim*, and *Tanya*—and that you add from time to time to your study of the teachings of *Chassidus*.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, *shlita*,

Secretary

## 5496

5496

B"H, 5 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn

To the esteemed Rabbi and Chossid, G-d-fearing and distinguished,
R' Avraham Tzvi, may he live, HaKohen

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your notification regarding your son, Yisrael Yitzchak, may he live, reaching the age of mitzvos:

May it be the will of Hashem that from the age of thirteen for mitzvos, he will grow to the age of fifteen, and so on, as the Mishnah rules (Avos, Chapter 5), and may he increase his diligence and perseverance in his Torah study—both in the revealed Torah and in the teachings of Chassidus—and be meticulous in the performance of mitzvos. May Hashem grant him success in becoming a Chossid, a G-d-fearing individual, and a scholar, and may you and your wife, may she live, derive from him and from all your offspring, may they live, an abundance of Chassidishe nachas.

With blessings for good tidings, including regarding the expansion—in both quantity and quality—of the shiurim in Chassidus that Divine Providence has granted you the merit to teach in public; for there is nothing that can stand before the will, and for the receiving of the Torah with joy and inwardness.

To note and illuminate: at the time of the Giving of the Torah, the hidden aspects of Torah were given in a revealed manner (Maaseh Merkavah, etc.), whereas the revealed parts of Torah were given only in a hidden and alluded manner. And it is known from our Sages, **"And He commanded us":** just as there [the hidden was revealed], so too here [the revealed is hidden].

See Torah Or 22b. See there.

From the ruling of the Alter Rebbe that one should not interrupt [during certain prayers], it is proven that it is an obligation.

## 599

B"H, 10 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter from Erev Shavuos, the time of the giving of our Torah, in which you write regarding what you heard from the doctor and the impression it made upon you and your wife, may she live.

It is surprising that you seem to have forgotten the saying of the Tzemach Tzedek regarding the precise phrasing of our Sages, "The Torah gave permission to the doctor to heal." He emphasized that he has permission to *heal*, but not, Heaven forbid, to cause despondency, etc. Especially in our times, when new medical treatments are being developed every day and new medicinal remedies are being discovered, it is contrary to sound judgment to make predictions of the sort you describe. I only wish that you would be strong in your trust in the Creator of the world and its Guide, who oversees each and every one with Divine Providence. In the near future, you will see tangibly that the aforementioned words were false, although it is understood that you must take some action through natural means—see also the *kuntres* and the *maamar* of this date regarding this—and therefore, you should also look into a vegetarian diet.

I was very pleased to hear that your wife, may she live, spoke before the women, and that this also had an effect on her health in the literal sense. This itself is proof to the contrary of the aforementioned doctor’s words; for the Jewish people are **one** nation on earth, and an improvement in spirituality must necessarily bring about an improvement in physicality as well.

With blessings to you and your entire household, may they live, to serve Hashem in all your ways, with joy and goodness of heart.

## 500

B"H, 10 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter from Erev Shavuos, the time of the Giving of our Torah.

It is understood that one must arrange study schedules while also maintaining physical health in the literal sense. There is sufficient time in the day and night for all of this, and if only one would utilize all the time remaining after attending to the body’s necessary needs! As is well known, the Rambam states that maintaining a healthy and sound body is among the ways of Hashem. Conversely, G-d forbid, if one is distracted—and in our generations, this is the primary concern—from the proper study of Torah and the fulfillment of mitzvos, the consequences are tangibly evident.

Regarding the *nigleh* study schedules in the yeshiva, it goes without saying that one should not miss them, for it is the duty and the privilege of every yeshiva student to uphold the yeshiva’s order.

Regarding your question about *Kiddush* and *Havdalah*, it is known that wine takes precedence over all other beverages, and in our camp, it is available. Furthermore, regarding what you wrote concerning *farbrengens*, and especially regarding *avreichim*, I do not see any benefit in intoxicating beverages, G-d forbid, in addition to the fact that they leave an impression that is entirely undesirable. It is known that my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, told a story about the custom of the early Chassidim, who would place a bottle on the table at the beginning of a *farbrengen* and simply look at it. Only in special cases would they require it, and even then, with extreme limitation. *Kal v'chomer*.

With blessings for good news regarding diligence and perseverance in Torah study, in good health,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

This only became known to me in recent years. You should examine the *sefer Mishnas Avraham*, section 32, which lists *Sifrei Torah* of holy individuals—and not specifically from Chabad—who were precise to write it with an *Aleph*, and likewise in my letter printed in *Otzar HaPoskim*, end of Part I.

## 503

B"H, 11 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

... Regarding your question: 1) The contradiction between the two statements—that one who sees a sotah in her degradation should abstain from wine, versus the statement that a nazir is called a sinner for depriving himself of wine—is resolved in the Tosafot, s.v. Amar (Taanis 11a).

## 519

B"H, 12 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn

To the Kfar Chabad Committee,
May Hashem preserve them and keep them alive.

Greetings and Blessings,

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your report dated the 19th and 22nd of Iyar. It is surprising that there is no discussion regarding several questions that should be on the agenda, as they are currently pressing issues. Among them (though not all) are the following:

According to information from several people, it has become known here that there are those who wished, or are currently wishing, to settle in Kfar Chabad. Given their nature, they are well-suited to the direction of Kfar Chabad. However, they received a response from the village administration that this is out of the question, as there are no houses available—and nothing more. It is understood that the village will not develop in such a manner.

In a situation like this, one should visit the appropriate institutions and make efforts to increase the number of houses slated for construction, or add to the houses currently being built—especially since several of those who wrote to me regarding this are prepared to contribute specific sums of their own. Yet, in your report, I find **no** mention of these requests or the responses given to them. Perhaps the reason is that, in your opinion, it is out of the question, as mentioned above. My perspective on this, however, is understood from what has been said: it is the complete opposite. At a time when other villages are making progress and seeing actual success—with people wanting to settle in Kfar Chabad—you are sitting idly by in this regard. Apparently, you are content with going through the list of houses that were approved for construction a year or two ago, and if they are occupied, you inform the applicant that there is nothing to discuss, and that is that!

This lack of action regarding the above also raises suspicion that perhaps the same applies to finding ways to stabilize the village and improve its economic situation, etc. It is imperative that you find ways to make a fundamental change in the aforementioned approach. May it be the will of Hashem that this change occurs very soon, for the success of Kfar Chabad in all matters, both materially and spiritually.

It is also surprising that you do not mention in your report whether you have already discussed the summer camp in Kfar Chabad. This is especially pertinent given that you have witnessed from previous years that discussing the camp only in the final moments before its opening has led to several shortcomings, both materially and spiritually.

With blessings for success in your holy work for the **development** of Kfar Chabad, and for good tidings regarding all of the above.

#### ***519***

From a copy.

## 5110

B"H, 12 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed Chossid, G-d-fearing and upright, involved in communal needs,
R' Alexander Sender, may he live.

Greetings and blessings!

...It is surely unnecessary to arouse you regarding the necessary increase in the dissemination of printed Chassidic materials, and all the more so regarding the matters of putting on tefillin and affixing mezuzos about which you write. This is much more than the concept of "ascending in holiness," for we see tangibly the saving of Jewish souls for the life of the World to Come when we arouse them through speech and connect that arousal to actual deed through practical mitzvos like tefillin, etc. This is in accordance with what is explained in Likutei Torah, that an arousal from Above must be connected to an arousal from below and in actual deed, and this is well-understood.

May it be the will of Hashem that you do all of this with the appropriate energy and in ever-increasing dimensions, with joy and a glad heart, and may you influence all those in your surroundings to do the same. The Giver of the Torah and the Mitzvos is certain to fulfill His promise that whoever adds, is added to in what they need, both materially and spiritually. It is simple that physical health, in the literal sense, is also included in this. After all, we have just come from the days of receiving the Torah, and as our Sages expound, at the time of the giving of the Torah, all of Israel were healthy and whole, even physically and in their bodies.

I believe that during our conversation I pointed out to you that, surely, during your travels, you find seforim in shuls and also in private homes that are lying there like a stone that no one turns over—if you will forgive the comparison—and are not being used. It would be worthwhile to send them here if they are rare or the like, and in their place provide the community with Siddurim, Chumashim, Kitzur Shulchan Aruch, and the like; the benefit is understood. It is surprising that you do not mention the above, although I hope that you have not forgotten the matter.

With blessings for good tidings.

As the holy Shelah writes, may your spiritual activities likewise correspond to this: to draw all the matters of the *nefesh habehamis* within the Jewish person and make them vessels for the matters of (in the language of our Rebbe, the Alter Rebbe) the "second soul" in a Jew, which is a literal part of Elokus from above, which is called, as it were, the *Adam HaElyon* (the Supernal Man) upon the Throne.

With respect and blessing for good tidings in all the aforementioned, in true health.

## 513

B.H., 14 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 28 Iyar, in which you write regarding the marriage proposal for your son, may he live.

It is understood and simple that a condition that is non-negotiable is the matter of the *sheitel*. It is obvious that the elderly women and those who came from your country in the past cannot be compared to a young couple now beginning to build a home in Israel, and in Kfar Chabad, which was founded and is directed by my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory. This is not merely a private matter, but also a general matter, a safeguard, and a merit for the public. On the other hand, I do not mean that it is necessary to speak about this with noise and anger, or as an order and command. The main thing is the practical outcome, and that they should be secure regarding this before the *chupah*. Given how you describe the nature of the young woman in question, and how she has taken upon herself the conduct of the Bais Yaakov school—and it seems she listens to the instructions of the principal there, who surely will also assist in the advocacy of this matter—I am confident that they will succeed in coming to an agreement regarding this. Especially since, as mentioned, this affects the many; even if a certain person says that regarding herself it makes no difference how she covers her hair, as long as the covering is as required, it is understood that she cannot guarantee this regarding others, and this is obvious. If this is arranged—which is the true benefit of both of them and their families—the proposal is appropriate, and may it be in a good and auspicious hour.

There is room to say that someone might argue regarding the conduct of the mothers, that it was not necessarily with a *sheitel*. But their words have already been addressed [based on what is known in Chassidus, that all the opposites of the blessings in the Torah are in truth blessings (see *Likutei Torah*, *Parshas Bechukosai*)]. This is one of the blessings of the *ikvesa d'meshicha*, that a daughter rises against her mother, etc., etc., to surpass her in the observance of Torah and *mitzvos*, and this is obvious.

With blessings for good news regarding the above, and for your health and livelihood,

**M. Schneerson**

## 514

B"H, 14 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and learned R' Yehuda, may he live.

Shalom u’Bracha!

In response to your letter and *pidyon nefesh* of the 10th of Sivan.

At an auspicious time, I will mention you and your family, may they live, at the holy *tziyun* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, for whatever you require, in accordance with the contents of your letter.

I was pleased to read at the end of your letter that you study *Chassidus* from time to time. It is my firm hope that from now on, this study will be consistent. And may it be that this consistency is not only in time, but also in the soul—so that this study is felt throughout the entire day through an added light and vitality in matters of *Torah* and *mitzvos*, and in general, in all of a person’s affairs, regarding which it is said, "In all your ways, know Him" (*Mishlei 3:6*). This is discussed at length in the *Hilchos Deios* of the Rambam and in the *Tur Shulchan Aruch, Orach Chaim*, section 231; see there.

Since you mention in your letter that you were present at the *farbrengen* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, which took place at the Pappenheim home in Vienna, I assume you are referring to that *farbrengen* where my father-in-law, the Rebbe, told the story of his grandfather, the Rebbe Maharash, traveling to Paris specifically to save and bring back to *teshuvah* one Jewish soul. This story is well-known among *Chassidim*. My father-in-law, the Rebbe, concluded then that the young man who was brought to *teshuvah* through that trip later became the head of one of the most *chareidi* Jewish families in France.

Since everything is by *hashgacha pratis*, including the fact that you mention in your letter that you were present at that *farbrengen*, this is certainly an instruction that you must engage energetically in *hafatzas hamaayanos chutzah* (spreading the wellsprings of *Chassidus* outward). Surely, Divine Providence will provide you with many opportunities, provided you truly desire it, and you will succeed in this dissemination. It is simple that through this, the blessings of Hashem will also increase for what you and your entire family, may they live, require, and regarding the matters you wrote about in the *pidyon nefesh*. I look forward to hearing good news.

With blessings.

P.S.
Surely you know of and observe the three *shiurim* that are for every person, as instituted by my father-in-law, the Rebbe—the *Chumash*, *Tehillim*, and *Tanya*—as is well known. In any case, you should observe them from now on.

## 5116

B"H, 15 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter, in which you ask for advice on how to extricate yourself from undesirable matters.

It is well known that first and foremost, one must divert one’s mind from the entire matter, even from the effort to rectify it (see also on this, *Responsa Tzemach Tzedek*, *Shaar HaMiluim*, section 62). Diverting one’s mind—as explained in many places in the *Gemara*—does not mean engaging in a battle against it, for that is the opposite of the concept of diverting one’s mind. Rather, it means diverting one’s thoughts to a different subject. Since the mind is always wandering, the diversion is achieved by shifting one’s thoughts to an entirely different matter—and how much better if it is a matter of the light of Torah, for even a little light pushes away much darkness.

And since for everything one requires *siyata d'shmaya*, in addition to the fact that you are surely careful regarding *tevilas Ezra*, it is appropriate that every weekday, before the morning *davening*, you set aside a few coins for *tzedakah*, as our Sages of blessed memory said: "Rabbi Eliezer would give a coin to a pauper and then *daven*." Furthermore, keep the three *shiurim* that are equal for every soul, as instituted by my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory—in *Chumash*, *Tehillim*, and *Tanya*—which are a *segulah* for many matters. It is understood that all of the above should be done *bli neder*, as is the custom in Israel today, and as is understood from what is written in *Likutei Torah*, *Parshas Mattos*, in the *drushim* regarding *neder*, and in *Sefer HaMitzvos* of the *Tzemach Tzedek* regarding the *mitzvah* of *nedarim*.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe,
Secretary

## 519

B"H, 15 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 8 Sivan, regarding your question on how to discern whether a thought is a suggestion from the *yetzer tov* or from the *yetzer hara*.

Since you have studied the story of the *tzaddik* of Chernobyl (which was brought in the *sicha* of Pesach 5703), and you will surely see what is also brought in *Hayom Yom* for 23 Sivan, this is the solution to your question.

Regarding your second question, whether you are permitted to study *Tanya* with *avreichim*: not only is it permitted, but you are obligated to do so. As is explained at length in many places, the study of the *pnimiyus haTorah* that has been revealed in our generation through the teachings of Chassidus is a *chovas gavra* (a personal obligation). This is because it concerns not merely knowing another subject in Torah, but it is also a source of strength for the manner in which one fulfills *mitzvos* and studies the *nigleh* (revealed) Torah. May you have good news to report regarding all of the above.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary

## 5220

B"H, 15 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 12 Sivan.

Regarding what you wrote concerning the names of a groom and his father-in-law, where each has two names and only one of them is shared by both:

It is known that our Rabbeinu HaZaken — author of the Tanya and the Shulchan Aruch — and his grandson, the Tzemach Tzedek, ruled that there is no room for concern here (see Responsa Tzemach Tzedek, Even HaEzer, siman 143, and Piskei Dinim of the Tzemach Tzedek, Shulchan Aruch Yoreh Deah, siman 116).

Regarding what you wrote concerning the reason why a woman would then call her husband by her father’s name, there is debate on this in several books. However, the Rambam writes that this is a name that is not common; see also Responsa Tzafnas Paneach (by the Rogatchover Gaon), siman 128, part 1, and in Sefer Otzar HaPoskim, part 1, page 100 and onwards.

With blessings.

## 5121

B"H, 17 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, learned, and diligent in communal affairs, R' Boruch, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

After a long silence, your letter from Sunday arrived, along with the enclosure, which—per your request—is being returned herewith.

Regarding your request for my opinion and comments: without entering into the details—which the lawyer will certainly utilize, and which he will review and edit to ensure it aligns with what he wishes to highlight for the judge—it is necessary to make the general observation that the entire second chapter should be removed. My reasoning for this is clear: since the first chapter presents the opinions of all the Orthodox rabbinical bodies, showing that they are unanimous in their view on the question addressed in the second chapter, it is superfluous to bring proofs for their opinion. More importantly, these proofs could actually be harmful, and the reason is understandable:

The opposing side can dispute every proof. Even if they cannot twist the proof itself, they will strive to find a counter-proof, and it is well known that "the gates of excuses are never locked." Furthermore, by the very act of bringing proof for the authority of the rabbinical bodies, two things emerge: a) that you yourself feel a need for something beyond the *psak din* of the Rabbis; and b) that you think the person sitting in court is the appropriate arbiter to declare whether the Agudas HaRabbanim, etc., is correct (a right which even the judge himself does not believe he possesses). Most importantly, the entire approach of asking a non-Jew (or even a Jew, *lehavdil*, who is not a *musmach*) whether he agrees with a *psak din* of a Rav is contrary to the *Shulchan Aruch*—it is the approach of Conservatism and Reform!

Instead of this second chapter, it would be better to provide precise dates of the *psak din* from the Agudas HaRabbanim, etc.—if possible, the original with an English translation—as well as evidence that there are no other united Orthodox rabbinical bodies besides those you list in that chapter. Connect this to the fundamental point upon which the lawyer must insist: that as an Orthodox shul, the only thing binding upon it is a *psak din* from Orthodox Rabbis, not from Reformers. Just as a *psak din* from priests is not binding upon the shul—even though the latter have a faith—the Reformers are *kofrim* in the Torah of Moshe and have no other Torah, *lehavdil* (for the word "Torah" itself, as recognized even by non-Jews, is bound up with *Torah min HaShamayim*).

I am pleased that you apply to yourself the analogy of "you shall make" (*miksha tei'aseh*), for by doing so you take upon yourself the responsibility. You cite the verses regarding the trumpets and the Menorah; the purpose of the trumpets was to gather the Jews through Moshe Rabbeinu, who received the Torah at Sinai, and the Menorah was to illuminate with the light of holy oil, not only in the Beis HaMikdash but, as Chazal say, in this world as well. The analogy is understood: "And all that comes out of his mouth he shall do." Stop arguing that this is not your affair; rather, take upon yourself the matter of the trumpets and the Menorah—hammer away and hammer away until the matter is accomplished. May it be the will of the Giver of the Torah, the Torah of Truth, who is also the Leader of the world in all places and at all times, that it should come to you much more easily than you anticipate, and with great success.

With blessings for good tidings.

## 5123

B"H, 18 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and distinguished individual, who engages in communal needs with integrity and energy, etc., R' Yaakov, may he live, Cohen.

Peace and Blessing!

I am writing to you in connection with the Lubavitcher shul, and I am simultaneously writing to the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and distinguished individual, who engages in communal needs, etc., R' Y.A., may he live, Levitansky.

The situation of the Lubavitcher shul in Chicago and the circumstances in which it finds itself have brought me to the firm conclusion that not only can the shul be sustained and continue to exist, but one can—and indeed must—strengthen and elevate the shul and expand all its activities and positive endeavors.

For this to happen, however, it is necessary that those who are connected to the shul, and to whom the matter of a Lubavitcher shul is close and dear, take action. It is highly self-evident that the *askanim* must consult with one another in ways of pleasantness and peace, and with one heart, regarding this work, and seek the ways and means to establish the Lubavitcher shul on the most proper footing and ensure its continued activity and development.

Both of you, together with the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and distinguished *chossid*, a man of fine character, etc., etc., the *Tamim* Rabbi Shlomo Zalman, may he live, Hecht—who have merited to hear from my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, many times regarding Lubavitcher matters in Chicago in general and in particular—must be among the first in this effort. You must also influence all the other members of the shul so that the aforementioned activity is carried out to the fullest extent and with the fullest cooperation in all details.

And it is certain that when you are harmoniously united with a firm resolution for the benefit of the cause, the *Ribono shel Olam* will send His blessing for extraordinary success.

Awaiting good news, and with blessings and regards to *anash*, may they live.

## 5724

B"H, 18 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter from the day on which it was doubled in goodness, in which you write regarding the incident—may we be spared—on Shabbos and your actions at that time due to the panic, and you write that your heart is pained over this.

First and foremost, you must ask for a halachic ruling from a Rav, for it is possible—on the contrary—that what you did was a mitzvah, as this depends on the specific details of the incident. In any case, based on what is written in the Rambam at the end of Hilchos Lulav, and based on what is written in many places in the holy Tanya regarding the absolute necessity in avodas Hashem to be specifically in a state of joy and goodness of heart—and avodas Hashem is as it is written, "In all your ways know Him," and as is the halachic ruling in Shulchan Aruch Orach Chaim, siman 231—you must accept this directive as well. May it be Hashem’s will that the saying of the holy ones of old be fulfilled in you, that "after a fire, one becomes wealthy," for this awakens the attribute of mercy, which is greater than the attribute of chesed that preceded it, which is the attribute of Yaakov, "an inheritance without boundaries," extending from one end to the other (see Derech Mitzvosecha by the Tzemach Tzedek, 114b).

With blessings for good tidings regarding the above.

#### ***5724***

## 5125

B"H, 18 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed, G-d-fearing, and learned man, who engages in communal needs, R' Yaakov, may he live.

Peace and blessing!

After the long hiatus, it was a pleasure to receive your letter of 12 Sivan with the good news regarding the improvement in [her] health... May it be His will that her condition continues to improve until she returns to full strength.

And from one good thing to another, [I received] your news regarding your efforts and success in expanding the Gemach fund in your synagogue.

It is worth noting the connection between these two matters: The function of a synagogue—a *beis tefillah*—is to request human needs before the Omnipresent, including the literal needs of *banai, chayei, u’mezonei revichei* (children, life, and ample sustenance). The vessel for drawing down the fulfillment of this request into the lowest levels—that is, the simple, literal matters of *banai, chayei, u’mezonei revichei*—is the mitzvah of *tzedakah*. This is explained in *Iggeres HaKodesh* by our Rebbe HaZaken in several places, and the highest level of *tzedakah* is the matter of *Gemach*. And this is well understood.

May it be His will that for many more years you continue to perform kindness through the aforementioned Gemach fund, as well as *gemilus chasadim* in spiritual matters, through the dissemination of Yiddishkeit in general and the dissemination of the *maayanos* in particular, wherever your hand can reach. This will also add to your success in the Gemach with this *aluvah* (see *Tanya*, beginning of Chapter 29)—*Gemach* both in physicality and in spirituality.

With a blessing in the phrasing of the Rebbe, my father-in-law, of blessed memory: for a healthy and happy summer.

#### ***5125***

**R' Yaakov:** Horowitz, Ramat Gan.

## 5126

With blessings to every member for success in this work, and also for success in her individual matters, so that you may grow to be worthy of bearing the name of Jewish daughters, daughters of Sarah, Rivkah, Rachel, and Leah. With blessings for good tidings.

**Sarah Mindel, may she live:** Gorlitsky (Shemtov), Montreal. Another letter to her — above, Vol. 11, 3531.

## 5127

B"H, 18 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter, in which you write that many have sought to marry you, but as of yet, nothing has come to fruition.

It is understood that you must not, Heaven forbid, be discouraged by this. You must resolve with the greatest firmness that when the Creator of the world and its Guide brings your proper *shidduch* to you, you will conduct your home upon the foundations of Torah and *mitzvos*, observing the *mitzvos* with *hiddur*. A true resolution in this matter will hasten the finding of your correct *zivug*.

Regarding what you write about what you were told concerning a witch, etc., you should completely dismiss this from your mind. The Creator of the world and its Guide watches over you, and witchcraft has no power whatsoever, Heaven forbid. As mentioned above, conduct yourself as is fitting for a kosher daughter of Israel—each of whom is called by the names of Sarah, Rivkah, Rachel, and Leah. Through this, you will increase the blessings of Hashem, the Giver of the Torah and the *mitzvos*, in all that you require in general, and for a good *shidduch* in particular.

And since you teach a class, you should also increase your influence on the conduct of the class regarding matters of *yiras shamayim* and the fulfillment of *mitzvos*, for this will bring about an increase in what you yourself require.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 5728

B"H, 18 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn, N.Y.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter of 29 Iyar, which has just been received, in which you write regarding a *yungerman* who is a *rosh yeshiva* currently standing at a crossroads, as he has two options before him:

a) To continue in his holy work as *rosh yeshiva* until now, though this consumes most of his time, leaving insufficient free time to flourish in his own learning, at a time when there are those who predict for him a brilliant future in the understanding and study of Torah.

b) To leave his position as *rosh yeshiva* and enter a *kollel* to sit and study Torah for himself, but in that case, the level of the *yeshiva* in general would decline, and the *yeshiva*—which is unique of its kind in the Holy Land, may it be rebuilt and established—would fall apart, as he is its central pillar.

It is understood that he must continue his holy work in the *yeshiva* where he serves as *rosh yeshiva*, and to increase his strength in this. The merit of the many—the students with whom he learns—will assist him also in what he needs for himself to flourish in Torah. As is known, the saying of our *Rabbeinu HaZaken*—the author of the *Tanya* and the *Shulchan Aruch*—in the beginning of his book *Torah Or*, is that through the act of *tzedakah*, one's mind and heart become refined a thousand times over. His aforementioned work is *tzedakah* in a spiritual sense, and *kal v'chomer* (it is understood).

I will take this opportunity to urge him to keep the three daily *shiurim* that are incumbent upon every soul, as instituted by my father-in-law, the Rebbe, *ztzuklalah, nishmaso b'eden, zechuso yagein aleinu*:

a) Reciting the monthly *Tehillim* *shiur*—as the *Tehillim* is divided for the days of the month—each day after *Shacharis* davening.

b) Studying the *Chumash* *parsha* with the commentary of *Rashi* for the weekly *sedra* (on Sunday, from the beginning of the *sedra* until the *Sheni* portion; on Monday, from *Sheni* until *Shlishi*, etc.).

c) Studying a *Tanya* *shiur* every day—as the *Tanya* is divided for the days of the year. These are a *segulah* for many matters.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, *shlita*,

Secretary

## 5130

B"H, 19 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

In response to your letter from Erev Shabbos, with the enclosed note for... *shiy*.

At an *eis ratzon*, I will mention you at the *tziyon hakadosh* of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, *ztzukulah"h, nbg"m, zya*, for whatever each of you requires. Surely you have asked Dr.... *shiy* regarding your ailment, to see if it is related to the circulation of blood in the leg—I mean that sometimes in the fine veins, which are further from the heart, the blood circulation is not as rapid as necessary.

Based on the above regarding the lower man in his body, the precision of the language of our Sages becomes more understandable: "sins that a person tramples with his heels" (according to the first explanation of Rashi, *Avodah Zarah* 18a). These require special supervision so that the action of the heart—the heart of a Jew, which even in *galusa* is awake to Torah and *mitzvos*—is felt within them. And *kal v'chomer*.

Surely you will make a detailed plan for the days of redemption, the upcoming 12th and 13th of Tammuz. This year is, in the language of the verse, "And it was in the thirtieth year"—see the introduction to *Kuntres* 76 regarding this.

With blessings for good tidings in all the above, with proper health.

P.S.
Surely he will also participate in the opening of the *mikveh* and the celebration. My intent in the word "participate" is also in accordance with the opinion of those *Acharonim* who explain the concept of partnership: that ownership applies to every single point, and there is no point where he is not an owner just like the other partners. That is to say, the partnership should be evident in all matters pertaining to it. May it be His will that in this, too, you bring good news.

## 5131

B"H, 19 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

I hereby acknowledge receipt of your letter from Monday, in which you write regarding your situation at Yeshivas Tomchei Temimim in...

Surely, for someone like you, it is superfluous to explain that the principle of refusing to hear only one side of an argument is not merely a scriptural decree, but a matter of logic. It is understood that even in a case such as this, this logic and reasoning remain valid, especially since there are two primary points to consider.

a) There are meetings of the Tomchei Temimim administration. As is evident from your own letter, you participated in the meetings where these matters were discussed. That was the time to raise the details you now mention in your letter. And even if we were to say that the difficulties that would arise did not occur to you at the time (he did not anticipate this), the principle that "the mouth that forbade is the mouth that permitted" is also logical. There is always room to raise these points in subsequent meetings and to discuss the matters with the participation of all members of the administration. Furthermore, the concept of "salvation through a multitude of counselors" is also logical, especially regarding questions of this nature.

b) The following point is primary—and this applies not only to the content of your letter but also to what others from the administration of Tomchei Temimim write: It is understood and simple that the goal of every yeshiva and its main purpose is the welfare of the students. The *Roshei Yeshiva*, the *Magidei Shiur*, and the yeshiva administration are the means through which the welfare of the students is realized in practice. From this, it is also understood simply that in every difficult matter in the yeshiva, one must first take into account the primary goal, namely, the welfare of the students, and what their welfare requires... From this, it is understood that it is imperative that the *Roshei Yeshiva* and *Magidei Shiur* exert themselves as much as is necessary, setting themselves aside (*on aveikleygin zich on a zeit*) for the sake of the students' welfare. **Only then** are they fulfilling their role faithfully. Moreover, it is **precisely** in such a manner that their own standing will also grow, since the *Roshei Yeshiva* and *Magidei Shiur* are elevated by the elevation of the yeshiva. As much as the yeshiva increases in quality and quantity and in the benefit to the students, the stature and standing of the *Roshei Yeshiva* and *Magidei Shiur* increase many times over—and not only in their spiritual standing and in a *seguli* (supernatural) sense, but also in a material sense, and by way of cause and effect, even in a natural way.

Clearly, all of the above is directed to each and every *Magid Shiur*, *Rosh Yeshiva*, and member of the administration. Since there is no time to debate with each one individually, you have the permission and the right to convey my perspective regarding the discussion—in clean language—that is taking place among them, along with all of the above. May it be the will that these words, which come from the heart, will enter the heart of the listener and have an effect upon him, similar to the *pan* that the Rebbe (Rashab) *n.e.* would write in connection with the recitation of a *maamar*, that it should have an effect on me and on the listeners, and this is simple.

With blessings for success in your holy work to raise students who will live up to their name, *Temimim*, with true breadth of mind.

## 5133

With blessings,

## 5734

21 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn

Greetings and blessings!

I read with great astonishment the letter you sent on the 10th of Sivan, regarding the "great trial" your brother (may he live) faced (by traveling on an Israeli ship on Shabbos). My astonishment stems from the coldness of your tone in writing about such an appalling matter. Even more so, I am troubled that you label this a "great trial," equating the situation to a mere challenge, when in fact we are discussing the public desecration of Shabbos—a transgression equivalent to all the mitzvos combined, to the point that his wine is forbidden, etc. And the "reward" for this desecration was that he would be present... at his sister’s (may she live) wedding! I did not sense that his desire to be present at the wedding was because he was so passionate about fulfilling the mitzvah of *hachnasas kallah*. I am certain that he, too, does not deceive himself into thinking that it was the importance of the mitzvah of *hachnasas kallah* that drove his desire to travel to Eretz Yisrael (may it be rebuilt and established) specifically in that manner.

As in all matters, it is understood that here, too, the principle of "one transgression leads to another" has been fulfilled. At first—to use your phrasing—it was due to a "lack of courage" (and it is understood how strange such an expression is regarding a transgression in general, and specifically regarding the transgression of public desecration of Shabbos). He traveled in a way that—as I told him clearly—constitutes public desecration of Shabbos. Consequently, he sanctified the Name over wine belonging to the owners of a ship that operates through the public desecration of Shabbos, which renders the wine forbidden to drink (and any *hashgachah* on this is a mockery, for the prohibition of the wine of one who publicly desecrates Shabbos is not due to the contact of a non-Jew. And this is simple). Furthermore, according to many great authorities, even a double seal (*chotam b'toch chotam*) is of no avail here, since the wine was already forbidden to drink from the outset. From this, he fell into the doubt of whether one should even recite a blessing over it, and as is known, there is a dispute regarding such prohibitions. In any case, this involves the prohibition of "one who blesses the greedy, blasphemes Hashem." From this, he was led into further doubt—whether he ate that night without *Kiddush*. Yes—he surely read or used in some other way the electric light on the ship, whose passengers and crew are members of the Jewish people; consequently, all light produced for their sake is forbidden to others as well, even regarding the laws of "a candle for one is a candle for a hundred." Likewise, this led to further issues regarding the drinking of water and *netilas yadayim*. On ships of our time, they take water from the sea and boil it to turn it into fresh water. From this, it is understood that the water used at night came from the boiling of the previous day, and similarly, the water used during the day comes from the boiling that occurred some hours prior—meaning that the fresh water on the ship in the middle of the day is that which they boiled during the night or the morning of Shabbos. In this case, the law of "a candle for one is a candle for a hundred" does not apply, and he transgressed many other prohibitions as well.

Even if, with Hashem’s help, this is (perhaps) called *shogeg* (unintentional), there is no room for such a defense regarding a *ben yeshivah*.

It is understood that as a brother and relative, at first glance, these expressions may seem harsh. But when one contemplates the matter for a brief moment *aliba d'emes* (in accordance with the truth), one will see that they are not at all commensurate with the **most** appalling matter that has occurred, and the darkness in which people find themselves, calling this a "great trial"—at a time when he could have sat and studied Torah in tranquility, and perhaps even in purity, at the yeshivah here. Instead, he traded this for a journey to the land upon which the eyes of Hashem are from the beginning of the year to the end of the year, and he did so in a manner that is the opposite of the mitzvah of Shabbos, which is equivalent to **all** the mitzvos. If a spark of *yiras shamayim* remains in him, let him seek a wise person who will give him a *tikkun* for this, and even then, perhaps, he might be able to squeeze through.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 5131

B"H, 21 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

...It is understood that in *shidduchim* there must be the full consent and good will of both parties involved. On the other hand, however, you should explain to your daughter, *tichyeh*, that in our times one must be particular about the essential matters—namely, *yiras shamayim*, Torah, and *mitzvos*, for this is the whole of man. Therefore, one should not add conditions regarding matters that are not as significant, since it is not always possible to find both [the essential and the secondary]. It is understood that the essential is the essential, and the secondary and the secondary-to-the-secondary are not.

When two of *bnei Yisrael* meet, it is understood that the results must be an increase in matters of Torah and *mitzvos*. Therefore, I am taking this opportunity to urge you regarding the greatest necessity of our times: to set times for the study of *Chassidus*, and specifically as written in your lineage. Do not be satisfied with your own study alone, but influence your surroundings as well, to spread the wellsprings of this Torah there. This is, of course, in addition to the three *shiurim* that are equal for every soul, established by my father-in-law, the Rebbe, *ztzukulleh"h, nivg"m, zya"a*, in *Chumash*, *Tehillim*, and *Tanya*. They are: 1) Reciting the monthly *Tehillim* *shiur*—as the *Tehillim* is divided for the days of the month, each day after *davening* *Shacharis*. 2) Studying the *Chumash* portion with the commentary of Rashi from the weekly *sedra* (on Sunday from the beginning of the *sedra* until the *sheni* portion, on Monday from *sheni* until *shlishi*, etc.). 3) Studying the daily *Tanya* *shiur* as it is divided for the days of the year.

With blessings for good news regarding the above.

## 5737

B"H, 24 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

To the esteemed and distinguished Rabbi Yosef, may he live:

Peace and blessing!

In response to the note you sent via our dear friend, the esteemed avreich Aharon, regarding the commentary in *Daas Zekeinim al HaTorah* on *Parshas Beha'aloscha* (11:27), citing the *Tosafos HaRosh*: "Eldad and Medad were brothers of Moshe, but when the Torah was given and all forbidden relationships were prohibited, everyone separated [from their wives]... and Amram, the father of Moshe, also separated from Yocheved, his aunt who was his wife, and married another woman, who then bore him Eldad and Medad..." You asked how this is possible, given that the selection of the elders described in *Parshas Beha'aloscha* took place shortly after the giving of the Torah, and at that time Eldad and Medad were prophesying in the camp.

The intention of the *Rosh* is that Amram’s separation from Yocheved was **for the reason** mentioned, but not **at the time** mentioned. Amram was a prophet, and when he became aware of the aforementioned prohibition, he separated from Yocheved. This is explained more explicitly in the book *Toldos Yitzchak* by Rabbi Yitzchak Karo in *Parshas Beha'aloscha*. He writes (as quoted in the supplement to *Torah Sheleimah*, *Parshas Shemos*): "Amram knew through Moshe of the prohibition of forbidden relationships that the Torah would eventually forbid, and he separated from Yocheved his aunt and took another wife."

It is my hope that you have fixed times for the study of Chassidus, the inner dimension of the Torah, which in the language of the holy *Zohar* is the *neshamah d'oraysa*—the soul of the revealed Torah, which is called the *gufa d'oraysa* (the body of the Torah), as is known from the *Zohar*, Vol. 3, 150a.

With respect and blessing,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 5140

B"H, 25 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and Blessings!

Following the longest of interruptions, your letter of 11 Sivan was received. Although it does not explain the reason for said interruption, it is understood that it causes distress that [Name]—may she live and be well—is not on close terms with [Name] and does not interact with them. This is despite the fact that it is understood, and indeed simple, that the opposite should be the case, and that this would be beneficial for both sides, as is evident in a tangible way. With a little reflection on the fact that the Ribono shel Olam created human beings such that their opinions are not identical, and yet we are commanded, "Love your fellow as yourself," it is clear that there is no contradiction at all between these two matters, as is also evident in a tangible way.

In most instances, the reason for such a situation is that one of the parties imagines a slight in the other party's attitude toward them. In their view, it would diminish their own honor to be the one to take the first step toward reconciliation, especially when they do not find within themselves any reason for such a slight in attitude. But as mentioned above, in the vast majority of cases, this is nothing more than an imagination, and when one begins the process of reconciliation, the error in this becomes clear immediately. May it be the will [of Hashem] that in this matter, too, there will be a noticeable improvement, and the sooner, the better.

Based on the above, it is also understood that all these things apply as well to the avodah in the vineyard of Chabad specifically, just as I have written many times to various members of anash, for I see in this a channel of blessing in the physical as well, not only in the spiritual.

Surely you are continuing to work on the matter regarding which you write, and I will await good news regarding all of the above.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,

Secretary

## 5141

...Since you write that you have been engaged in teaching for many years, and surely you contemplate the hearts of the youth, it is certainly unnecessary to remind you that if at all times and in all places the motto and goal of the Jewish educator must be to educate students according to the verse, "Fear G-d and keep His mitzvos, for this is the whole man," then this is all the more so in our time and in the land called by all the nations of the world the Holy Land. This is the central and inner point of the education of the children of Yisrael—namely, that regardless of what subject is being studied with the students or in what manner, there must be engraved in the mind and heart of the educator the aspiration to instill in his students, even through that specific study, the "fear of G-d," in a way that will be expressed in their daily lives, and "the keeping of His mitzvos."

And surely the merit of your ancestors assists you, consistent with your writing that you are a grandson of the Rebbe of Gostynin, who is known and famous for his love for the children of Yisrael and for his fear of Heaven, and the merit of the many assists you.

## 5742

B"H, 25 Sivan, 5717
Brooklyn.

Greetings and blessings!

In response to your letter with the *pidyon nefesh* that will be read at an auspicious time at the holy resting place of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, of blessed memory, may his merit shield us.

In it, you write regarding [name]—may he live—that you are at a loss regarding how to conduct yourself with him.

The advice of our Sages is already well-known: "Always push away with the left and draw near with the right," and this applies to a child. In his specific case, you should consult with a psychologist, since many times—and perhaps in the majority of cases—behavior such as that which you describe in your letter is related to nervous tension or the like, and it is often within the doctor's power to provide significant help in this regard. In any event, as it appears from your description, expelling him from the home could, *chas v'shalom*, lead to a worsening of the situation rather than a correction, and the wise will understand. May it be Hashem's will that you will soon be able to share good news regarding the above. It is understood and simple that the more you, the writer, and the members of the family in general, increase your involvement in matters of Torah and *mitzvos*, the more you will be blessed by Hashem in general, and specifically in the fulfillment of your request regarding the above.

With blessings,

On behalf of the Rebbe, shlita,
Secretary
